Clarification: The Three refers to Mecca, not Medina
Author clarifies that 'the three' exclusively refers to Mecca, not Medina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'the three' in this context refers to Mecca and not Medina, and how the definition of 'residence' as birthplace resolves a potential objection.

Core Idea
What is meant by 'the three' is Mecca, the more honorable city.

In this passage, the author comments on a previous statement that 'the three' refers to Mecca. He explains why the third place is not Medina: because Mecca is more honorable due to Prophet Ismail's settlement. He also clarifies that 'residence' means birthplace, so Ismail's residence does not contradict the intended meaning. This clarification prevents a misunderstanding about which city is intended.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the author's precise language: 'والمراد بهذه الثلاثة مكة'.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that 'the three' refers to Mecca. Note that he does not specify which three, but the context is about places of birth or residence.
3
Understand the objection
The author explains why the third place is not Medina: because Mecca is more honorable due to Prophet Ismail's settlement. Ask: why would someone think it's Medina?
The objection likely arises from a different interpretation of 'the three'.
4
Grasp the resolution
The author clarifies that 'residence' means birthplace. Thus, Ismail's residence in Mecca does not contradict because he was born elsewhere. This shows the importance of precise definitions.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this clarification prevents a misunderstanding. How does defining 'residence' as birthplace resolve the potential conflict?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والمراد بهذه الثلاثة مكة شرفها الله تعالى لم يحمل الأخير على المدينة لأن مكة التي حتد بها إسماعيل عليه السلام أشرف من المدينة، وأكرم عند الجمهور ثم المراد من الإقامة بطريق الولادة فلا نقض بإسماعيل عليه السلام وسنبينه على توجيه آخر.
His statement: (And what is meant by these three is Mecca, may Allah honor it) – he did not apply the last (i.e., the third) to Medina, because Mecca, which Ismail, peace be upon him, settled in, is more honorable than Medina, and more noble according to the majority. Then what is meant by 'residence' is by way of birth, so there is no refutation by Ismail, peace be upon him, and we will explain it on another interpretation.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification. The author comments on a previous statement that 'the three' refers to Mecca. He explains that the commentator did not apply the third (presumably a third place) to Medina because Mecca, where Prophet Ismail settled, is more honorable. The term 'residence' is understood as birthplace, so the fact that Ismail lived there does not contradict because he was not born there. The author promises a fuller explanation later.

Key Terms
الثلاثة
مكة
المدينة
الإقامة
الولادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'the three' includes Medina as the third place.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'the three' refers exclusively to Mecca, and that the third place is not Medina because Mecca is more honorable and 'residence' means birthplace, so Ismail's settling in Mecca does not conflict.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the third place is not Medina?
A) Because Medina is not a place of birth
B) Because Mecca is more honorable due to Ismail's settlement
C) Because the hadith mentions only Mecca
D) Because the Quran specifies Mecca

Key Takeaway
The term 'the three' in this context refers to Mecca, not Medina, because of Mecca's greater honor and the definition of residence as birthplace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious statement that seems clear, do you ever stop to question whether your initial understanding might be based on an assumption rather than the precise wording? How can you develop the habit of checking the original intent before acting on it?
?
The author distinguishes 'residence' by birth from mere dwelling. In your own life, do you confuse familiarity with authority—assuming that because you are 'resident' in a practice (e.g., praying a certain way) it is the only correct way? What step can you take today to verify your practices against the scriptural or scholarly intent?

Defining the Quran: The Specific Composition, Not Individual Speech
Commentator defines the Quran as a specific composition, not individual speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran is defined as a specific composition that remains the same across all recitations, and how this resolves apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
The Quran is the eternal composition, not the temporal recitation.

In this passage, the commentator tackles a critical question: What exactly is the Quran? Some might think it is the speech that came to Jibril or exists with Allah. The commentator argues that this cannot be, because that would make each recitation a different entity. Instead, he defines the Quran as the specific composition that remains unchanged regardless of who recites it. This distinction safeguards the Quran's unity while accounting for its temporal recitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the decisive tone: 'اعلم أن الحق أن...'
2
Identify the main claim
The commentator claims that the Quran is not the individual speech (الشخص الحقيقي) subsisting with Jibril or Allah, but rather the specific composition (المؤلف المخصوص).
3
Follow the proof
Understand why the Quran cannot be individual speech: accidents are individuated by their loci, so if it were individual speech, each recitation would be similar, not identical. Therefore, it must be the composition that does not differ with different reciters.
The example of books and poems is used to generalize the principle.
4
Understand the clarification
The commentator explains that the claim of 'eternity of expressions' means the expressions subsist in God's essence eternally, not that our utterances are eternal. Our recitations are temporal because accidents are tied to their loci.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الحق أن القرآن ليس اسماً للشخص الحقيقي القائم بلسان جبريل عليه السلام، أو بالله تعالى خاصة، للقطع بأن كل ما يقرأ كل واحد منا هو القرآن المنقول عن النبي عليه السلام بلسان جبريل عليه السلام، ولو كان عبارة عن ذلك الشخص لكان هذا مماثلاً له لا عينه ضرورة أن الأعراض تتشخص بمحالها فتتعدد بتعدد المحال، بل هو عبارة عن هذا المؤلف المخصوص الذي لا يختلف باختلاف المتلفظين، وكذا الكلام في كل كتاب أو شعر ينسب إلى أحد، فمراد من ادعى قدم الألفاظ أنه لم يوجد زمان لم تتحقق معه هذه الألفاظ ضرورة قيامها بذاته تعالى أزلا، وإلا فالألفاظ القائمة بنا من حيث إنها كذلك فله الحدوث ضرورة حدوث الحال بحدوث المحل والقول بأن القائم بنا هو القراءة لا المقروء مما لا يلتفت إليه فتأمل.
Know that the truth is that the Qur'an is not a name for the individual real speech subsisting upon the tongue of Jibril, peace be upon him, or specifically with Allah the Exalted, because it is certain that everything that each one of us recites is the Qur'an transmitted from the Prophet, peace be upon him, upon the tongue of Jibril, peace be upon him. If it were that individual speech, this [what we recite] would be similar to it, not identical, necessarily because accidents are individuated by their loci and thus multiply by the multiplicity of loci. Rather, it is this specific composition that does not differ with the difference of reciters. And similarly is the case in every book or poem attributed to someone. So the meaning of whoever claims the eternity of the expressions is that there was no time when these expressions were not realized, because of their necessary subsistence in His Essence eternally. Otherwise, the expressions subsisting in us, inasmuch as they are such, are temporal necessarily because of the temporality of the accident with the temporality of its locus. And the statement that what subsists in us is the recitation, not the recited, is something not to be paid attention to. So reflect.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a crucial definition of what the Qur'an is. The commentator argues that the Qur'an is not the individual speech (al-shakhṣ al-ḥaqīqī) that exists specifically upon the tongue of Jibril or with Allah alone. The reason is that each one of us recites the same Qur'an, which is transmitted from the Prophet through Jibril. If the Qur'an were that individual speech, then what we recite would be merely similar to it, not identical, because accidents (such as speech) are individuated by their loci (the one who speaks). Different loci produce different instantiations. Therefore, the Qur'an must be the specific composition (al-muʾallaf al-makhṣūṣ) that remains one and the same regardless of different reciters. This applies to any book or poem attributed to an author: the work is the composition itself, not the individual utterances. The commentator then explains what is meant by those who claim the eternity of the expressions (qidam al-alfāẓ): they mean that the expressions (as they subsist in God's essence) have always been real, not that the expressions we utter are eternal. Our own utterances are temporal because they are accidents in a temporal locus. The commentator dismisses the view that what subsists in us is only the recitation (qirāʾa) and not the recited (maqrūʾ), calling it a view not worth considering. This is a fine point distinguishing the eternal speech of God from its temporal recitation by creatures.

Key Terms
القرآن
كلام الله
قديم
عبارات
متلفظين
مؤلف مخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is the individual speech that Jibril or Allah spoke, so each recitation is that same individual speech.
Correction: If the Quran were individual speech, each recitation would be a similar but different accident, not identical. The Quran is the specific composition that remains one regardless of reciter.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why cannot the Qur'an be the individual speech subsisting with Jibril or Allah?
A) Because the Qur'an is created.
B) Because if it were, each recitation would be similar but not identical, since accidents are individuated by loci.
C) Because Jibril's speech is temporal.
D) Because Allah's speech is not in a language.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the specific, eternal composition that remains the same across all recitations, while our recitations are temporal accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly feel that you are uttering the eternal speech of Allah, or do you treat it as just another book? What would change if you internalized that every letter you recite is a temporal manifestation of the eternal composition?
?
The commentator dismisses the view that what we recite is only recitation, not the recited. Do you ever feel disconnected, as if your recitation is not really 'the Quran'? How can you overcome that feeling and believe that your recitation is indeed the Quran itself?
?
If the Quran is the specific composition that does not differ with reciters, then every recitation is equally the Quran. Does that make you more careful in your recitation? How can you increase your presence when reciting, knowing that you are reproducing the eternal words?

The Quran's Protection from Corruption
Sanusi lists three protections for the Quran and provides a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran is protected from falsehood, abrogation, and textual alteration, and why the imamate is attached to principles.

Core Idea
The Quran is divinely protected from all corruption.

The author begins by affirming the absolute protection of the Quran. He argues that falsehood cannot approach it from any direction, its rulings are not subject to abrogation, and its text remains free from alteration, unlike previous scriptures. This lesson lays the foundation for the Quran's inviolability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author uses a defensive tone.
2
Identify the three forms of protection
The passage mentions three ways the Quran is preserved: from falsehood, from abrogation, and from textual alteration. Locate each in the text.
3
Reflect on the proof from the Quran
The author cites verse 15:9 as proof. Ask yourself why this verse is sufficient evidence for preservation.
This verse is a direct divine guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من (خلفه لا يجد إليه الباطل سبيلاً من جهة من الجهات إلا أنه خص هاتين الجهتين، لأن من يأتي شيئاً يأتيه غالباً من قدامه أو من خلفه، ( ولا يتطرق إليه (نسخ أي لا ينتهي حكمه بعد زمانه عليه السلام، وذلك لانقطاع الوحي وتقرر أحكامه إلى يوم القيامة ) ولا تحريف في أصله بأن تبدل كلماته عن مواضعها، كما فعلت اليهود بكلم التوراة ( أو وصفه ) بأن يغير مثلاً إعرابه أو تشديده كما غيرت النصارى تشديد ما أنزل إليهم في الإنجيل، من قوله : ولد الله عيسى من جارية عذراء أي جعله متولداً منها وإنما لم يتطرق إلى القرآن تحريف أصلاً لقوله تعالى : ( وإنا له لحافظون ﴾ [ الحجر : ٩] ، ولما توفاه ) إشارة إلى مباحث الإمامة فإنها وإن كانت من فروع الدين إلا أنها ألحقت باصوله دفعاً لخرافات أهل البدع بذاته تعالى ففهم الناقل من لفظ المعنى ما يقابل اللفظ أعني الكلام النفسي، وتوهم لذلك أن العبارات حادثة عند الشيخ فنقل كما فهم لا أن الشيخ صرح بحدوث العبارات واعلم أن الحق أن القرآن ليس اسماً للشخص الحقيقي القائم بلسان جبريل عليه السلام، أو بالله تعالى خاصة، للقطع بأن كل ما يقرأ كل واحد منا هو القرآن المنقول عن النبي عليه السلام بلسان جبريل عليه السلام، ولو كان عبارة عن ذلك الشخص لكان هذا مماثلاً له لا عينه ضرورة أن الأعراض تتشخص بمحالها فتتعدد بتعدد المحال، بل هو عبارة عن هذا المؤلف المخصوص الذي لا يختلف باختلاف المتلفظين، وكذا الكلام في كل كتاب أو شعر ينسب إلى أحد، فمراد من ادعى قدم الألفاظ أنه لم يوجد زمان لم تتحقق معه هذه الألفاظ ضرورة قيامها بذاته تعالى أزلا، وإلا فالألفاظ القائمة بنا من حيث إنها كذلك فله الحدوث ضرورة حدوث الحال بحدوث المحل والقول بأن القائم بنا هو القراءة لا المقروء مما لا يلتفت إليه فتأمل.
Falsehood cannot approach it from before it or from behind it; falsehood finds no way to it from any direction, but He specified these two directions because whoever approaches a thing mostly approaches it from its front or from its back. And it is not subject to abrogation, i.e., its ruling does not end after his time, peace be upon him, due to the cessation of revelation and the establishment of its rulings until the Day of Resurrection. And there is no alteration (tahrif) in its original text by changing its words from their places, as the Jews did to the words of the Torah, nor in its description, such as changing its i'rab or its tashdid, as the Christians changed the tashdid of what was revealed to them in the Gospel, from His saying: 'Allah begot Jesus from a virgin girl' meaning He made him generated from her. And alteration did not reach the Quran at all due to His saying, 'Indeed, We will preserve it' (Quran 15:9). And when he passed away, it is a reference to discussions of the imamate, for although they are branches of religion, they have been attached to its principles to repel the myths of the people of innovation. By His essence, exalted, the transmitter understood from the word 'meaning' that which is opposed to the word, namely inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi), and thus he imagined that the expressions are created according to the Shaykh, so he transmitted as he understood, not that the Shaykh explicitly stated the temporality of expressions. Know that the truth is that the Quran is not a name for the actual entity subsisting in the tongue of Gabriel, peace be upon him, or especially in Allah, because it is certain that everything that each one of us recites is the Quran transmitted from the Prophet, peace be upon him, by the tongue of Gabriel. If it were that entity, then this would be similar to it, not identical, because accidents are individuated by their loci and thus multiply by the multiplicity of loci. Rather, it is this specific composition that does not differ with the difference of utterers. Similarly, the case is for every book or poem attributed to someone. So the intention of one who claims the pre-eternity of the words is that there was no time in which these words were not actualized due to their subsistence in His essence eternally. Otherwise, the words subsisting in us inasmuch as they are such are originated necessarily due to the origination of the accident with the origination of its locus. And the saying that what subsists in us is the recitation (qira'a) not the recited (maqru') is not to be paid attention to. So reflect.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument defending the Quran from various forms of corruption and clarifying its nature as the eternal speech of Allah. The author begins by asserting the complete protection of the Quran from falsehood entering it from any direction, emphasizing that falsehood cannot approach it from front or back. He then explains that the Quran is immune to abrogation (naskh) of its rulings after the Prophet's time because revelation has ceased and its rulings are established until Judgment Day. Next, he denies that any textual alteration (tahrif) has occurred in the Quran, unlike the alterations made by Jews to the Torah (changing words) and Christians to the Gospel (changing diacritical marks). The proof that the Quran is preserved is the verse 'Indeed, We will preserve it' (15:9). He then transitions to a discussion of the imamate, noting that although it is a branch of religion, it is attached to the principles to refute heretical myths. Returning to the Quran, he discusses a misunderstanding: a transmitter interpreted the word 'meaning' in the sense of inner speech (kalam nafsi) and mistakenly thought that expressions were created according to the Shaykh (probably al-Ash'ari). The author clarifies that the Quran is not the actual speech subsisting in Gabriel's tongue or in Allah's essence exclusively, because each recitation is the Quran transmitted from the Prophet, and if it were that entity, each recitation would be merely similar, not identical, because accidents are individuated by their loci. Rather, the Quran is the specific composition that remains the same across different utterers. He then explains that those who claim the pre-eternity of the words mean that these words have always been actualized in Allah's essence, while the words subsisting in us are originated because they are accidents. The claim that what subsists in us is the recitation (qira'a) rather than the recited (maqru') is dismissed. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms
القرآن
الباطل
النسخ
التحريف
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran could be textually altered over time.
Correction: The Quran is divinely protected from any alteration, as proven by Quran 15:9 and the lack of tahrif in history.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a form of protection for the Quran in this passage?
A) Protection from being abrogated
B) Protection from falsehood
C) Protection from being memorized incorrectly
D) Protection from textual alteration

Key Takeaway
The Quran is divinely safeguarded from falsehood, abrogation, and alteration, as affirmed by Allah's promise in Surah Al-Hijr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt even a flicker of doubt that the Quran you hold today is exactly as revealed? This passage tells you that falsehood cannot approach it from any direction—so where does your doubt come from? Confront it now.
?
The Quran's rulings are unchangeable until the Day of Resurrection. Is there a command in the Quran you are tempted to treat as outdated or no longer applicable? What would it cost you to submit to it fully starting today?
?
You believe the Quran is preserved, but does your life reflect that trust? When you read it, do you receive it as the unaltered speech of Allah, or do you read it with the same casualness as any human book? How will you change that today?

The Nature of the Quran as Eternal Speech
Sanusi refutes a misinterpretation and defines the Quran as a specific composition, discussing its pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran is not merely the speech subsisting in Gabriel or Allah, but a specific composition that is the same across reciters, and that the words in Allah's essence are pre-eternal while our recitations are originated.

Core Idea
The Quran is the eternal speech of Allah, identical across all recitations.

The author clarifies a misunderstanding about the Quran being only inner speech (kalam nafsi) and addresses the claim that expressions are created. He argues that the Quran is not the actual entity in Gabriel's tongue but the specific composition that remains unchanged regardless of the reciter. He also distinguishes between the pre-eternity of words in Allah's essence and the temporality of our recited words.

Read the passage carefully

This sets the stage for the author's own view.
3
Grasp the argument about the Quran's identity
The author argues that the Quran is not the actual entity in Gabriel's tongue but a specific composition. Follow the reasoning about accidents and loci.
This is a key point in kalam theology.

What Is Kalam?

Imamate: Branch of Religion or Principle? A Nuanced Distinction
Gloss on the imamate's classification as branch vs principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the imamate is considered a branch of religion in its specific rulings yet touches on principles of religion regarding divine obligation.

Core Idea
The imamate is a branch of fiqh but its denial of God's obligation makes it a matter of usul.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a subtle classification: is the imamate a branch (far') or a principle (asl) of religion? The commentator navigates between two perspectives, showing that while the appointment of imams is a communal obligation by revelation, the question of whether God is obligated to appoint them falls under divine attributes. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the chosen view to the alternative perspective.
The passage is a gloss on the main text, so 'قوله' refers to the author's statement.
2
Identify the two classifications
Look for where the passage says 'من فروع الدين' and 'من أصول الدين'. Note the reason for each classification.
The chosen view makes it a branch; the alternative considers it a principle due to the negation of obligation upon God.
3
Understand the communal obligation
Find the phrase 'نصب الإمام واجب على الأمة سمعاً'. This clarifies that the appointment is obligatory on the community by revelation, not on God.
'أهل الحق' refers to Sunni scholars.
4
Connect to the subject of kalam
The passage references al-Armawi's view that the subject of kalam is God's essence. Consider how the imamate discussion relates to that.
This shows that the imamate is relevant to both fiqh and kalam.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'فليتأمل' meaning reflect. Think about the nuance: the imamate is a branch in its particulars but touches on the principle of divine non-obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن كانت من فروع الدين على ما هو المختار من عدم كون وجوب الإمام منصوباً من عند الله تعالى فلا يكون نصب الأئمة من الصفات الفعلية وقد يجعل من أصول الدين باعتبار أن انتفاء وجوب نصب الإمام على الله من أحكامه تعالى كما سنشير إليه فيما ينقل عن الأرموي من أن موضوع الكلام هو ذات الله تعالى وأنت خبير بأن نصب الإمام واجب على الأمة سمعاً عند أهل الحق فمباحث الإمامة من حيث خصوصها من الفروع المتعلقة بأفعال المكلفين، وأما انتفاء وجوبه عليه تعالى فمندرج في مسألة أن الله تعالى لا يجب عليه شيء فليتأمل .
His saying: 'Even if it (the imamate) is from the branches of religion according to the chosen position that the obligation of the imam is not appointed by God, then the appointment of imams is not among the essential attributes. And it may be considered from the principles of religion in consideration that the negation of the obligation of appointing the imam upon God is from His rulings, as we will indicate in what is transmitted from al-Armawi that the subject of kalam is the essence of God Almighty. And you are well aware that the appointment of the imam is an obligation upon the community by revelation according to the people of truth. So the discussions of imamate in terms of their particularity are from the branches related to the actions of those under obligation. As for the negation of its obligation upon Him, it is included in the issue that God is not obligated to do anything. So let it be considered.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a gloss (قوله) on a phrase in the main text discussing the status of the imamate. It distinguishes two aspects: first, whether the imamate is from the branches (فروع) of religion or from the principles (أصول). According to the chosen view (المختار), the obligation of the imam is not appointed by God (i.e., it is not a divine command that is part of God's essential attributes). Therefore, the appointment of imams is not among God's essential attributes (الصفات الفعلية). However, it can be considered a matter of usul al-din (principles of religion) in the sense that the denial of God's obligation to appoint an imam is a ruling about God (i.e., that He is not obligated). This relates to the doctrine that nothing is obligatory upon God. The passage also references al-Armawi's view that the subject of kalam is God's essence. The author then clarifies that the imamate is, in fact, an obligation upon the community (الأمة) by revelation (سمعاً) according to Ahl al-Haqq (Sunni scholars). Therefore, discussions of imamate in their particular details are part of the branches of fiqh pertaining to the actions of responsible individuals. But the broader issue of whether God is obligated to appoint an imam falls under the principle that God does not do anything out of obligation. The passage ends with 'falyatammal' (so let it be considered), indicating the nuance.

Key Terms
فروع الدين
أصول الدين
نصب الإمام
الصفات الفعلية
وجوب
أرموي
موضوع الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imamate is solely a branch of religion with no relation to principles.
Correction: The imamate involves both: its particular rulings are branches, but the question of God's obligation is a matter of usul al-din because it pertains to a ruling about God.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why can the imamate be considered part of usul al-din?
A) Because the appointment of imams is an essential attribute of God.
B) Because the negation of God's obligation to appoint an imam is a ruling about God.
C) Because it is obligatory by revelation.
D) Because it is part of fiqh.

Key Takeaway
The imamate is classified as a branch of religion in its details, but the issue of divine obligation makes it relevant to the principles of religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever quickly classified a religious issue as a 'branch' or 'detail' without considering its deeper implications about God? What does this say about your desire for intellectual convenience over seeking truth?
?
When you hear debates about leadership in the Muslim community, do you feel a personal stake in ensuring that your understanding of the issue aligns with the principles of faith? Or do you tend to sidestep difficult theological questions?
?
If the imamate's classification affects how you view God's obligation, then how does your view of God's freedom affect your own sense of duty? Are you willing to let your actions be guided solely by revelation, even when your intellect might resist?

Two Types of Perfections and Intra-Species Variation
Defines two kinds of perfections and applies the second to intra-species variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between passive and active perfections, and how the latter determines superiority among individuals of the same species.

Core Idea
True excellence lies not in what you have, but in what you emanate.

This passage from Sanusi's introduction addresses a subtle but important point: perfection is not a single category. There are perfections that belong to a thing without producing any effect, and perfections that manifest in intentional actions. The second kind, according to Sanusi, explains why some individuals tower above others within the same species, as illustrated by a striking poetic comparison.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the two definitions and the concluding poetry.
The passage uses a technical vocabulary: 'قائمة به' (attached to it) and 'صادرة عنه' (emanating from it).
2
Identify the two perfections
The passage explicitly lists two kinds. Find the key phrases: 'قائمة به به غير صادرة عنه' and 'آثار صادرة عنه مقصودة منه'. Understand each.
The first is like knowledge in a human; the second is like writing from a human or cutting from a sword.
3
Reflect on the variation argument
Sanusi argues that variation among individuals is due to the increase/decrease of the second perfection. Consider the poetry: one person counted as a thousand, another as heaven vs. earth. What does this imply about human excellence?
This connects to the broader theme of kalam: rational judgments about perfection and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قائمة به به غير صادرة عنه، كالعلم للإنسان مثلاً . والثاني آثار صادرة عنه مقصودة منه ،بخصوصه، فتختص به أيضاً كالكتابة الصادرة عنه وكالمضاء للسيف . وبحسب زيادة ذلك المذكور، أعني الكمال الثاني، ونقصانه بفضل بعض أفراده بعضاً إلى أن يعد أحدهم بألف : ولم أر أمثال الرجال تفاوتت إلى المجد حتى عد ألف بواحد . بل يعد أحدهم سماء والآخر أرضاً : الناس أرض بكل أرض وأنت من فوقهم سماء
What is attached to it but not emanating from it, like knowledge for example. And the second is effects emanating from it, intended, specifically, so it pertains to it as well, like writing emanating from him and cutting for the sword. And according to the increase and decrease of that mentioned, I mean the second perfection, some of its individuals surpass others until one of them is counted as a thousand: And I have not seen men's likes differing in nobility until a thousand counts as one. Rather one of them is counted as heaven and the other as earth: People are earth on every earth, and you above them are heaven.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines two kinds of perfections (كمالات). The first kind consists of attributes that are attached to a thing but do not emanate from it, like knowledge for a human. The second kind consists of effects that do emanate from the thing intentionally and are specific to it, like writing from a human or cutting from a sword. The author then explains that variation among individuals of the same species is due to the increase or decrease of this second perfection. He illustrates this with poetry: some individuals are so superior that one is worth a thousand, and another is compared to heaven while others are earth. This shows that the real distinction among individuals lies in their active perfections, not in the essential, non-emergent attributes shared by all.

Key Terms
كمال
آثار
القوة النطقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All perfections are equal and vary equally among individuals.
Correction: Only the second kind of perfection (active effects) varies; the first kind does not vary in a way that causes superiority.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what determines the superiority of one individual over another within the same species?
A) The first kind of perfection, which is essential and non-emergent.
B) The second kind of perfection, which consists of intentional effects.
C) Both kinds of perfection equally.
D) The species itself, not individual variations.

Key Takeaway
The second kind of perfection — intentional, emanating effects — is the true measure of excellence and the source of variation among individuals of the same species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about the 'emanations' of your own life—your writing, speech, actions, and how they affect others. Are you actively producing intentional effects that benefit those around you, or are you merely possessing knowledge that stays locked inside? What one specific, deliberate action can you take today to increase your emanative excellence?
?
The poem says some people are like 'heaven' and others like 'earth'. In which area of your life are you currently 'earth'—passive, unproductive, or merely existing? What would it take for you to rise to be 'heaven' through intentional, beneficial actions? Be concrete.
?
If all your knowledge and attributes were suddenly removed except what you actively emanate, what would remain of you? What would people see? Are you satisfied with that, and what step can you take today to close the gap?

The Science of Kalam: The Highest and Most Beneficial Science
Declares kalam as the highest science and defines its subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical scholars considered the science of kalam the most important and beneficial science, and what subject matter it covers.

Core Idea
Kalam is the foundation upon which all other religious sciences rest.

After establishing the impossibility of mastering all sciences, the author turns to the question of which science deserves our utmost dedication. He argues that the science of kalam (theology) is the highest in rank, the most beneficial, and the most deserving of our ambition. This passage defines kalam as the discipline that proves the existence of the Creator, His unity, His attributes, and prophethood, and it asserts that all other religious sciences depend upon it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally before you interpret.
2
Identify kalam's three main topics
Notice that the passage describes kalam as covering: (1) proving the Maker and His unity, (2) establishing prophethood, and (3) providing the basis for all other religious sciences like tafsir, hadith, and fiqh.
This tripartite structure shows why kalam is considered the foundational discipline.
3
Reflect on the dependency of other sciences
Consider why the author says that without kalam, other sciences like tafsir and hadith would not be conceivable. How does proving the Creator and His attributes make other knowledge possible?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان الأمر على ما ذكر من تعذر الإحاطة بجملة العلوم ( فإذا الواجب على العاقل الاشتغال بالأهم وما الفائدة فيه أتم هذا كما ذكر ( وإن أرفع العلوم ) مرتبةً ومنقبة ) وأعلاها ) فضيلةً ودرجةً ( وأنفعها ) فائدةً ( وأجداها ) عائدة ( وأحراها ) أي أجدرها ( بعقد الهمة بها، وإلقاء الشراشر عليها ) يقال : ألقى عليه شراشره أي نفسه بالكلية حرصاً ،ومحبةً، وهي في الأصل بمعنى الأثقال شرشرة ) وإدآب النفس ) إتعابها ( فيها ) وتعويدها بها ( وصرف الزمان إليها علم الكلام المتكفل بإثبات الصانع وتوحيده في الألوهية ) وتنزيهه عن مشابهة الأجسام) ترك الأعراض إذ لا يتوهم مشابهته إياها ) واتصافه بصفات الجلال والإكرام أي بصفات العظمة والإحسان إلى المخلصين من عباده أو بالصفات السلبية والثبوتية أو القهر واللطف ( وإثبات النبوة التي هي أساس (الإسلام بل لا مرتبة أشرف منها بعد الألوهية ( وعليه مبنى الشرائع والأحكام أي وعلى علم الكلام بناء العلوم الشرعية، والأحكام الفقهية، إذ لولا ثبوت الصانع بصفاته لم يتصور علم التفسير والحديث، ولا علم
And since the matter is as mentioned, of the impossibility of encompassing all the sciences, (then the duty upon the rational person is to occupy himself with the most important and that whose benefit is most complete, as mentioned. (And indeed the highest of sciences) in rank and honor, (and the loftiest of them) in virtue and degree, (and the most beneficial of them) in benefit, (and the most fruitful of them) in return, (and the most deserving of them) i.e., most worthy (of directing one's ambition toward it, and casting one's entire concern upon it) — it is said: 'he cast his sharashir upon it' meaning his whole self eagerly and lovingly, and originally sharashir means burdens (sharshara) — (and fatiguing the soul) tiring it (in it) and habituating it to it, (and devoting time to it) is the science of kalam, which undertakes to prove the Maker and His unity in divinity, and to purify Him from resemblance to bodies (leaving aside accidents since resemblance to them is not imagined), and to describe Him with attributes of majesty and generosity — i.e., attributes of greatness and beneficence to the sincere among His servants, or the negative and positive attributes, or severity and gentleness — and to establish prophethood, which is the foundation of Islam, nay, there is no rank nobler than it after divinity. And upon it is based the divine laws and rulings, i.e., upon the science of kalam is built the religious sciences and jurisprudential rulings, for if the existence of the Maker with His attributes were not established, the sciences of tafsir and hadith would not be conceivable, nor the science...
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage concludes that since one cannot master all sciences, the rational person must choose the most important one. It then declares that the highest, most beneficial, and most deserving science to which one should dedicate all efforts is the science of kalam (theology). The passage defines kalam as the discipline that proves the existence of the Creator, His unity in divinity, His transcendence above resemblance to bodies, His attributes of majesty and generosity, and establishes prophethood. It argues that all other religious sciences (tafsir, hadith, fiqh) depend on kalam because without establishing the Creator and His attributes, these sciences would have no foundation.

A) Fiqh
B) Tafsir
C) Kalam
D) Hadith

Key Takeaway
Kalam is the foundational science that establishes the existence and attributes of God and prophethood, upon which all other Islamic sciences are built.

?

The Superiority and Definition of Ilm al-Kalam
Al-Iji defines ilm al-kalam and establishes its supremacy over other sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ilm al-kalam is considered the highest science and what its core topics are.

Core Idea
Ilm al-kalam is the queen of Islamic sciences because it establishes the foundations upon which all others rest.

This passage from al-Mawaqif defines ilm al-kalam and boldly declares its superiority. The author, al-Iji, argues that kalam is the most worthy of our full attention because it secures the very basis of faith and the other religious sciences. Let's delve into his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the emphatic opening: 'وإن أرفع العلوم...'
2
Identify the topics of kalam
The passage lists the subjects that ilm al-kalam covers. Find each one: proof of the Maker, His oneness, transcendence, attributes, and prophethood.
These five topics are the core of kalam.
3
Understand the argument for superiority
The author argues that other religious sciences (tafsir, hadith, fiqh) depend on kalam. Reflect on why that would be the case.
Without establishing the Creator, other sciences have no foundation.
4
Appreciate the goal of certainty
Kalam elevates faith from imitation to certainty. This certainty leads to success in both worlds.
The ultimate aim is not just intellectual satisfaction but spiritual and practical success.
5
Note the concluding imperative
The passage ends with 'so it is necessary to give this science all attention.' Consider why the author feels so strongly.
This is a call to prioritize kalam study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أرفع العلوم مرتبةً ومنقبة وأعلاها فضيلةً ودرجةً وأنفعها فائدةً وأجداها عائدة وأحراها بعقد الهمة بها وإلقاء الشراشر عليها علم الكلام المتكفل بإثبات الصانع وتوحيده في الألوهية وتنزيهه عن مشابهة الأجسام واتصافه بصفات الجلال والإكرام وإثبات النبوة التي هي أساس الإسلام وعليه مبنى الشرائع والأحكام إذ لولا ثبوت الصانع بصفاته لم يتصور علم التفسير والحديث ولا علم الفقه وأصوله وبه يترقى في الإيمان باليوم الآخر من درجة التقليد إلى درجة الإيقان وذلك الإيقان هو السبب للهدى والنجاح في الدنيا والفوز والفلاح في العقبى فوجب يعتنى بهذا العلم كل الاعتناء
And the highest of sciences in rank and honor, and the loftiest in virtue and degree, and the most beneficial in benefit, and the most rewarding in return, and the most worthy of focusing one's ambition upon and casting one's whole soul onto it, is the science of kalam which guarantees the proof of the Maker and His unity in divinity, and His transcendence above resemblance to bodies, and His being described with attributes of majesty and beneficence, and the proof of prophethood which is the foundation of Islam, and upon it are built the divine laws and rulings, for were it not for the establishment of the Maker with His attributes, the sciences of Quranic exegesis, hadith, and jurisprudence and its principles could not be conceived, and through it one ascends in faith in the Last Day from the level of imitation to the level of certainty, and that certainty is the cause of guidance and success in this world and victory and prosperity in the Hereafter, so it is necessary to give this science all attention.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines ilm al-kalam and establishes its superiority over all other sciences. The author begins by stating that the highest, most virtuous, most beneficial, and most deserving of complete dedication is the science of kalam. He then defines the scope of this science: it undertakes (1) proving the existence of the Creator and His oneness in divinity, (2) transcending Him above resemblance to bodies, (3) attributing to Him attributes of majesty and beneficence, and (4) proving prophethood, which is the foundation of Islam. He argues that all religious sciences—tafsir, hadith, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh—depend on the establishment of the Creator and His attributes, which kalam provides. Furthermore, kalam elevates one's faith in the afterlife from blind imitation to certainty, and this certainty leads to guidance and success in this world and the next. Therefore, it is obligatory to give this science the utmost care and attention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ilm al-kalam is merely speculative theology with no practical benefit.
Correction: This passage shows that kalam is foundational for all religious sciences and elevates one's faith to certainty, with benefits in this life and the next.

Checkpoint

B) The proof of prophethood
C) The afterlife

Key Takeaway
Ilm al-kalam is the highest science because it establishes the existence and attributes of Allah and prophethood, upon which all other religious sciences depend, and it raises faith from imitation to certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in Allah, but on what foundation does your faith rest? If it is mere imitation (taqlid) of your upbringing, will you commit this very week to study ilm al-kalam to raise it to certainty (yaqin)—even if it requires effort and humility? How else will you be among those who succeed in the Hereafter?
?
Al-Iji says that without kalam, tafsir, hadith, and fiqh are inconceivable. You likely engage with these sciences regularly. Have you ever felt the weight of their dependence on kalam? Starting today, will you give kalam its proper place in your learning, or will you continue to treat it as optional?
?
Certainty (yaqin) is described as the cause of guidance in this world and victory in the next. Look now at your own heart: do you possess that deep, unshakable certainty? If not, what specific one step will you take this week to move from borrowed faith to personal conviction through the study of kalam?

The Purpose of Kalam: From Imitation to Certainty
Author explains that kalam elevates from imitation to certainty, key to success.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam elevates one from blind imitation to firm certainty about the Hereafter, and why that certainty is essential for success in this life and the next.

Core Idea
Kalam transforms imitation into certainty, securing guidance here and salvation hereafter.

In this opening passage, the author establishes the foundational purpose of kalam: to move beyond mere tradition and achieve personal certainty about the afterlife. He argues that this certainty is the basis for true success in both worlds, making the study of kalam an essential religious obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the rise to certainty
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the emphasis on aṣ-ṣuʿūd (ascent) from taqlīd to yaqīn.
The parenthetical inserts are part of the commentary; they clarify the author's words.
2
Understand taqlīd vs. yaqīn
Taqlīd is accepting belief without proof; yaqīn is firm conviction based on evidence. The author says kalam enables the rise from one to the other.
3
Reflect on the consequences of certainty
Certainty is the cause of guidance and success in this world and victory in the Hereafter. Therefore, kalam is not optional—it is obligatory.
Connect this to the author's call to pay full attention to this science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المؤلف
الفقه وأصوله ( وبه يترقى في الإيمان باليوم الآخر من درجة التقليد إلى درجة الإيقان وذلك ) الإيقان ( هو السبب للهدى والنجاح في الدنيا ( والفوز والفلاح) في العقبي أن فوجب يعتنى بهذا العلم كل الاعتناء ) وإنه في زماننا هذا قد اتخذ ظهرياً ) أي أمراً منسياً قد ألقي وراء الظهر ) وصار طلبه عند الأكثرين شيئاً فرياً) بديعاً عجيباً، وقيل: مصنوعاً مختلقاً (لم يبق منه من علم الكلام ) بين الناس إلا قليل، ومطمح نظر من يشتغل به على الندرة قال وقيل هما فعلان والمعنى أن منتهى ما يرتفع إليه نظر من يشتغل به نادراً هو النقل عن شخص معين أو مجهول من غير التفات إلى دراية واستبصار في رواية ( فوجب علينا أن نرغب طلبة زماننا في طلب التدقيق، ونسلك بهم في ذلك العلم مسالك التحقيق
Introduction of the author. Fiqh and its principles (and by it one rises in faith in the Last Day from the level of imitation to the level of certainty, and that) certainty (is the cause of guidance and success in this world and victory and salvation) in the Hereafter, so one must pay full attention to this science. (And indeed in our time it has been taken as a back [i.e., forgotten]) i.e., a forgotten thing cast behind the back (and its pursuit has become for most people something strange) wondrous, and it is said: fabricated (nothing remains of the science of kalam) among people except a little, and the aspiration of whoever occupies himself with it, rarely, is naught but transmission from a specific person or an unknown one without attention to knowledge and insight in narration. (So it is incumbent upon us to encourage the students of our time in seeking precision, and to lead them in that science along the paths of verification).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an introduction in which the author establishes the importance of the science of kalam and laments its neglect. He begins by stating that through fiqh and its principles one ascends from blind imitation to certainty in belief in the Last Day, and that certainty is the cause of temporal and eternal success. Therefore, this science deserves full attention. However, in the present age, it has become neglected—an object cast behind the backs—and its pursuit is considered strange or even fabricated. Only a little of it remains among people, and those who do engage with it rarely go beyond transmitting from someone without genuine insight. Consequently, the author declares his duty to encourage students to seek precision and to guide them along the paths of verification. This opening sets the purpose of the commentary: to revive serious study of kalam.

Key Terms
تقليد
إيقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Blind imitation (taqlid) is sufficient for faith in the Hereafter.
Correction: The author asserts that one must rise from imitation to certainty through kalam; taqlid alone is inadequate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does kalam help one achieve in matters of the Last Day?
A) Blind imitation
B) Certainty
C) Confusion
D) Rejection

Key Takeaway
Kalam is the path to certain faith, which is essential for true success in this world and the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says certainty is 'the cause of guidance and success in this world.' Is your daily life guided by certainty about the unseen, or are you mostly driven by worldly concerns?
?
If you truly believed with certainty that the Last Day is coming, what would you change about how you spend your time today? Will you start that change now?

The Neglect of Kalam and the Call to Revive It
Author laments kalam's neglect and vows to guide students toward rigorous investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's diagnosis of kalam's neglect in his time and his mission to revive precise, verified study.

Core Idea
Kalam was cast aside; the author resolves to lead students back to precision and verification.

After establishing kalam's importance, the author laments its current state: it has become forgotten, its pursuit deemed strange or fabricated, and its practitioners only parrot authorities. He then declares his duty to rekindle careful investigation and verification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on neglect
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the strong imagery: 'taken as a back' (forgotten), 'strange', 'fabricated'.
The parenthetical explanations are part of the commentary.
2
Understand the critique of transmission
The author criticizes that kalam has been reduced to mere transmission from specific or unknown people, without insight or understanding.
3
Recognize the author's call to action
The author states his duty: to encourage students in seeking precision (tadqiq) and to lead them along paths of verification (tahqiq).
This sets the agenda for the entire commentary.

Source Text

مقدمة المؤلف
الفقه وأصوله ( وبه يترقى في الإيمان باليوم الآخر من درجة التقليد إلى درجة الإيقان وذلك ) الإيقان ( هو السبب للهدى والنجاح في الدنيا ( والفوز والفلاح) في العقبي أن فوجب يعتنى بهذا العلم كل الاعتناء ) وإنه في زماننا هذا قد اتخذ ظهرياً ) أي أمراً منسياً قد ألقي وراء الظهر ) وصار طلبه عند الأكثرين شيئاً فرياً) بديعاً عجيباً، وقيل: مصنوعاً مختلقاً (لم يبق منه من علم الكلام ) بين الناس إلا قليل، ومطمح نظر من يشتغل به على الندرة قال وقيل هما فعلان والمعنى أن منتهى ما يرتفع إليه نظر من يشتغل به نادراً هو النقل عن شخص معين أو مجهول من غير التفات إلى دراية واستبصار في رواية ( فوجب علينا أن نرغب طلبة زماننا في طلب التدقيق، ونسلك بهم في ذلك العلم مسالك التحقيق
Introduction of the author. Fiqh and its principles (and by it one rises in faith in the Last Day from the level of imitation to the level of certainty, and that) certainty (is the cause of guidance and success in this world and victory and salvation) in the Hereafter, so one must pay full attention to this science. (And indeed in our time it has been taken as a back [i.e., forgotten]) i.e., a forgotten thing cast behind the back (and its pursuit has become for most people something strange) wondrous, and it is said: fabricated (nothing remains of the science of kalam) among people except a little, and the aspiration of whoever occupies himself with it, rarely, is naught but transmission from a specific person or an unknown one without attention to knowledge and insight in narration. (So it is incumbent upon us to encourage the students of our time in seeking precision, and to lead them in that science along the paths of verification).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an introduction in which the author establishes the importance of the science of kalam and laments its neglect. He begins by stating that through fiqh and its principles one ascends from blind imitation to certainty in belief in the Last Day, and that certainty is the cause of temporal and eternal success. Therefore, this science deserves full attention. However, in the present age, it has become neglected—an object cast behind the backs—and its pursuit is considered strange or even fabricated. Only a little of it remains among people, and those who do engage with it rarely go beyond transmitting from someone without genuine insight. Consequently, the author declares his duty to encourage students to seek precision and to guide them along the paths of verification. This opening sets the purpose of the commentary: to revive serious study of kalam.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author condemns all transmission in kalam.
Correction: He condemns blind transmission without understanding, not transmission per se. He himself transmits from earlier authorities but insists on insight and verification.

B) Mere transmission without insight

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you seek knowledge, do you stop at transmission—accepting what someone says without grasping its evidence? ?
The author committed to leading students toward verification. Who are you responsible for guiding—yourself, your family, your peers? How will you encourage someone this week to seek precision in understanding their faith, rather than settling for what is 'familiar'?

The Inadequacy of Existing Kalam Works
Author describes his survey and critique of existing kalam books.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's assessment of prior kalam literature and the significance of his linguistic analysis of the word 'rawāʾ'.

Core Idea
Existing kalam books failed to cure the thirst for doctrinal truth.

The author begins by stating his thorough survey of all available kalam books. He found none that truly satisfied the seeker of sound doctrines. This critique sets the stage for his own contribution. The passage also includes a detailed linguistic discussion of the word 'rawāʾ' to demonstrate precision.

Read the opening statement where the author declares his survey and disappointment with existing works.

The concluding lines link back to the science of kalam and the proof of the Maker.

This passage is the motivation for the entire book.

وإني قد طالعت ما وقع إليَّ من الكتب المصنفة في هذا الفن فلم أر فيها ما فيه شفاء لعليل بأمراض الأهواء في الآراء (أو رواء) أي رو أو إرواء الغليل ( لحرارة العطش بفقدان المطالب الاعتقادية، والشوق إليها، وفي الصحاح أن الرواء بالمد وفتح الراء هو الماء العذب وبكسرها جمع ريان، وبضمها المنظر الحسن (سيما) حذف منه كلمة لا لكثرة الاستعمال والجملة الحالية أعني قوله : (أو رواء) في تاج البيهقي والصراح روي يروي رياً بالكسر والفتح، وروي كرضي سيراب شدن فهو في الأصل مقصور مده المصنف، ليناسب شفاء على ما نقل عن سيبويه، أن الألف الممدودة في الأصل مقصورة زيدت قبلها ألف لزيادة المد ثم قلبت الألف همزة، ثم إنه إما بمعناه الأصلي كما هو الظاهر، أو بمعنى المتعدي فإنه قد يستعمل المصدر اللازم بمعنى المتعدي كما في قوله تعالى والله أنبتكم من الأرض نباتاً ﴾ [نوح: ١٧ ] وإلى التوجيهين أشار الشارح بقوله : أي روى أو إرواء وقوله : وفي الصحاح بواو العطف إشارة إلى توجيه آخر وهو أنه يجوز أن يكون بالفتح بمعنى الماء العذب أي القاطع للعطش، أخر لفوات التناسب بقوله شفاء، فإن الظاهر حينئذ دواء بدل شفاء.
علم الكلام إلخ، وقد دل على أن المراد هذا بقوله : إذ لولا ثبوت الصانع إلخ حيث لم يقل إذ لولا إثبات الصانع بدليله، ولا شك في هذا الابتناء وكفايته في مدح الفن.
And I have examined what came to me of the books composed in this art, and I did not see in them what contains a cure for the sick of the diseases of passions in opinions, or a quenching, i.e., drinking or quenching of the intense thirst from the loss of doctrinal demands and longing for them. And in al-Ṣiḥāḥ: al-rawāʾ with elongation and fatḥa of the rāʾ is sweet water, and with kasra it is the plural of rayān, and with ḍamma it is beautiful appearance. (Sīmā) the word lā is omitted from it due to frequent usage, and the circumstantial clause, I mean his saying: (or rawāʾ) in Tāj al-Bayhaqī and al-Ṣirāḥ: rawā yarwī riyyan with kasra and fatḥa, and rawā like raḍiya means 'to be watered'; so it was originally maqṣūr, the author lengthened it to suit shifāʾ, according to what was transmitted from Sībawayh, that the elongated alif was originally shortened, an alif was added before it for extra extension, then the alif was turned into a hamza. Then either in its original meaning as is apparent, or in the transitive sense, for the intransitive infinitive may be used in the transitive sense as in His statement: 'And Allah caused you to grow from the earth as a plant' [Nūḥ: 17]. And the commentator indicated the two directions by his saying: i.e., watering or quenching. And his saying: 'and in al-Ṣiḥāḥ' with the conjunction wāw indicates another direction: that it may be with fatḥa meaning sweet water, i.e., the cutter of thirst, but he delayed it due to lack of appropriateness with his saying 'shifāʾ', for the obvious then would be 'dawāʾ' instead of 'shifāʾ'. The science of kalam etc., and it has indicated that what is meant is this by his saying: 'for if not for the existence of the Maker etc.' where he did not say 'for if not for the proof of the Maker', and there is no doubt in this foundation and its sufficiency in praising the art.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the author's critique of existing books on kalam. He states that after reviewing the available works, he found none that provide a cure for the disease of following whims in opinions or that quench the intense thirst for doctrinal matters. The rest of the passage is a detailed linguistic discussion of the word 'rawāʾ' (quenching), explaining its meanings, derivations, and how it is used here metaphorically. The author cites lexicographical works and grammatical authorities to justify the usage. He then returns to the main point: the science of kalam itself, noting that the reference to 'the existence of the Maker' indicates the subject matter is the proof of the Creator, and that this foundation is sufficient to praise the art.

The Necessity of Defining the Science
Sanusi presents the first objective and explains the necessity of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is essential to define a science before studying it, and how a proper definition provides comprehensive insight (basira) that prevents blind pursuit.

Core Idea
A proper definition gives the student comprehensive insight and distinguishes the science from others.

Sanusi begins by stating the first of six objectives: the definition of the science of kalam. He argues that presenting the definition is necessary so that the student has insight (basira) into what they seek, enabling them to grasp the science comprehensively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall meaning, then refer to the translation.
2
Identify the reason for presenting the definition
Look for the phrase that explains why the definition must be presented first. The key term is 'basira' (insight).
The text says: 'لطالبه على بصيرة'
3
Note the two types of definition mentioned
The passage mentions two ways to define a science: a proper definition (hadd) and a description (rasm). Both provide comprehensive coverage.
4
Understand the contrast with other conceptions
Sanusi contrasts definition with other ways of conceiving the science. Other ways may suffice but do not give the same level of insight.
The text says: 'بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأحق في طرق التعليم ) وفيه مقاصد ) ستة أيضاً ( الأول تعريفه ) أي تعريف العلم الذي يطلب تحصيله وإنما وجب تقديم تعريفه ليكون طالبه على بصيرة ) في طلبه فإنه إذا تصوره بتعريفه سواء كان حداً لمفهوم اسمه، أو رسماً له، فقد أحاط بجميعه إحاطة إجمالية باعتبار أمر شامل له يضبطه ويميزه عما عداه بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره فإنه وإن فرض أنه يكفيه في طلبه لكنه لا يفيده بصيرةً فيه (فإن من ركب متن العماية بمعنى الباطل ( أوشك أن يخبط خبط عشواء ) وهي الناقة التي لا عمياء) وهي قوله : (بمعنى الباطل) وهو هاهنا التصور بغير التعريف من الوجه الأعم، أو الأخص شبهه بالمركوبة في كون كل منهما سبباً لسلوك طريق الرسول إلى المطلوب، وأثبت المتن والركوب ففي الكلام استعارة بالكناية، وتخييل وترشيح وإنما قال : أوشك لأنه بمجرد التصور المذكور لا الشروع مشتملاً على فوائد الأمور الستة فلا شك أن الشروع بمثل هذه البصيرة موقوف عقلاً على الأشياء الستة، فيكون وجوب تقديمها أيضاً عقلياً والجواب أن توقف الشروع بالبصيرة المخصوصة عقلاً على الأمور المذكورة، إنما يوجب تقديمها على الإطلاق أعني ابتداء من غير تقييد بشيء، إذا كان الشروع بتلك البصيرة واجباً عقلياً على الشارع في العلم، من حيث هو طالب وهذا ظاهر على أنه يمكن أن يقال : المراد مطلق الشروع بالبصيرة، والمراد بتوقفه على الأمور المذكورة توقفه على نوعها كما حققناه في حواشي المطول . : له) قوله : ( سواء كان حد المفهوم اسمه أو رسماً (له قال رحمه الله تعالى : لا يخفى عليك أن اسم كل علم موضوع بإزاء مفهوم إجمالي شامل ،له فإن فصل في تعريفه ذلك المفهوم نفسه، كان حداً له بحسب اسمه وإن بين لازمه كان رسماً له بحسب اسمه، وعلى التقديرين هو : رسم لذلك العلم مميز له عن غيره وأما حده الحقيقي فإنما هو بتصور مسائله بل بتصور التصديقات المتعلقة بها، وليس ذلك من مقدمات الشروع . قوله: بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره فإنه وإن فرض أنه يكفيه في طلبه لكنه لا يفيده بصيرةً فيه) أراد بغيره غير التعريف، وغير التعريف يحتمل أن يكون وجهاً أعم، وكونه كافياً في طلب العلم الخاص من حيث خصوصه محل ،تردد فلهذا أورد قوله : وإن فرض إلخ لأن الكلام في الغير المطلق الشامل للاعم، وقوله : لكنه لا يفيد بصيرةً كاملةً يحصل بالتعريف، ثم الكلام في التصورات التي يمكن تقديمها على الشروع كما هو الظاهر من السياق، فلا يرد أن التصور الحدي للعلم باعتبار الحقيقة غير ما ذكر مع أنه على تقدير فرض كفايته في الطلب، ليس مما لا يفيد البصيرة، وذلك لأنه لا يحصل إلا بعد تمام تحصيل العلم المشروع فيه . قوله: (فإن من ركب إلخ هذا في موقع التعليل لإيجاب تصور العلم بتعريفه ليحصل البصيرة، ثم إن انتفاء هذا التصور المخصوص قد يكون بانتفاء أصل التصور، ولظهور عدم إمكان الشروع بدونه لم يتعرض له، وقد يكون بتصور لا يفيد البصيرة المذكورة، كالتصور بوجه أعم، وهو الذي أشار إليه بقوله : فإن من ركب إلخ .
And the most appropriate in the methods of teaching — and in it are also six objectives: the first is its definition, i.e., the definition of the science that one seeks to master. Indeed, it is obligatory to present its definition so that the student has insight into his pursuit. For if he conceives of it through its definition, whether it is a proper definition of the concept of its name or a description of it, he has encompassed all of it with a comprehensive coverage by virtue of a matter that encompasses it, governs it, and distinguishes it from others. Unlike if he conceives of it through something else, for even if it is assumed that it suffices him in his pursuit, it does not give him insight into it. (For whoever rides the mount of 'al-ʿamāyah' meaning falsehood is likely to stumble erratically like a blind camel). That is his statement: 'meaning falsehood,' and here it is the conception without definition from a more general or more specific aspect; he likened it to a mount in that each is a cause for traversing the path to the desired goal. And he affirmed the mount and the riding, so in the expression there is a metaphor by way of implicitness, imagery, and elaboration. And he only said 'likely' because by that conception alone, the beginning comprising the benefits of the six matters does not [result]. So there is no doubt that beginning with such insight is rationally dependent on the six matters; thus, the obligation to present them is also rational. The answer: that the rational dependence of the beginning with the specific insight on the mentioned matters only necessitates their absolute presentation—meaning starting without being restricted by anything—if the beginning with that insight is rationally obligatory on the starter in the science, insofar as he is a seeker. This is apparent, but it can be said that the intended meaning is the absolute beginning with insight, and the intended meaning of its dependence on the mentioned matters is its dependence on their type, as we have verified in the footnotes of al-Muṭawwal. His statement: 'whether it is a proper definition of the concept of its name or a description of it.' He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is not hidden from you that the name of every science is placed in correspondence with a comprehensive concept that encompasses it. So if he specifies in its definition that concept itself, then it is a proper definition with respect to its name; and if he explains its necessary concomitant, then it is a description with respect to its name. In both cases, it is a description of that science, distinguishing it from others. As for its real definition, it is only through conceiving its subject matters, nay, through conceiving the affirmations related to them, and that is not from the preliminaries of beginning. His statement: 'Unlike if he conceives of it through something else, for even if it is assumed that it suffices him in his pursuit, it does not give him insight into it.' By 'something else' he means other than the definition. And 'other than the definition' can be a more general aspect, and its sufficiency for seeking the specific science with respect to its specificity is a matter of doubt. Hence he invoked his statement 'even if it is assumed, etc.' because the discourse concerns the absolute 'other' that includes the more general. And his statement 'but it does not give him complete insight that is obtained by definition' — then the discourse concerns conceptions that can be presented before the beginning, as is apparent from the context. Therefore, it is not objected that the conceptual definition of the science with respect to reality is different from what is mentioned, and that assuming its sufficiency in the pursuit is not something that does not give insight, because it is not obtained except after complete mastery of the science in which one has begun. His statement: 'For whoever rides, etc.' — this is in the position of a cause for the obligation of conceiving the science through its definition in order to obtain insight. Then the absence of this specific conception may be due to the absence of the conception altogether, and due to the obvious impossibility of beginning without it, he did not address it. Or it may be a conception that does not give the mentioned insight, like conception from a more general aspect, and that is what he pointed to by his statement: 'For whoever rides, etc.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This long passage covers the first objective (المقصد الأول) which is the definition of the science (تعريفه). It explains why the definition must be presented: so that the student has insight (بصيرة) into what he is studying. If he conceptualizes the science through its definition (whether a true definition of its name or a description), he grasps it in a comprehensive manner. The passage then gives the simile of a rider on a blind camel (متن العماية) which illustrates the danger of proceeding without proper understanding. It then addresses an objection regarding rational obligation, arguing that the obligation to present these matters is not rational but conventional. Subsequently, the passage elaborates on the nature of definition: the definition can be either a proper definition (حد) of the concept of the name or a description (رسم). The real definition of the science, however, is through its subject matter and propositions, but that is not part of the preliminaries. The passage also discusses the phrase 'بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره' (unlike if he conceptualizes it otherwise), explaining that other conceptions may be too general or too specific and do not provide full insight. Finally, it notes that the loss of proper conceptualization may stem from lacking any conception or from having one that does not yield insight, with the latter being the point of the rider simile.

Key Terms
تعريف
بصيرة
تصور
حد
رسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any preliminary understanding is enough for studying a science.
Correction: The passage insists on a precise definition that provides comprehensive insight (basira) and distinguishes the science from others.

Checkpoint
Why is it necessary to present the definition of a science before studying it?
A) Because it is a religious requirement
B) To give the student comprehensive insight and prevent blind pursuit
C) Because it is part of the science's subject matter
D) To make the study easier

Key Takeaway
A proper definition of the science is presented first to give the student comprehensive insight that prevents blind pursuit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a new subject in Islam, do you stop to find a clear definition that gives you comprehensive insight, or do you dive in with a vague understanding? How has that affected your certainty in what you learn?
?
Think of a time you pursued knowledge without a proper definition. Did you later realize you lacked insight? What would change if you started with a definition as Sanusi advises?
?
If your understanding of a religious science is only partial and lacks the ability to distinguish it from other sciences, how might that lead to errors in your practice? Will you commit to seeking definitions first from now on?
