The Blind Camel Analogy and the Rational Obligation
Sanusi provides the blind camel analogy and discusses the rational obligation of the definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy of the blind camel used to illustrate the danger of studying without a proper definition, and the argument about rational vs. conventional obligation.

Core Idea
Studying without a definition is like riding a blind camel—likely to stumble blindly.

Sanusi employs a powerful analogy to reinforce the necessity of a definition: whoever proceeds without proper insight is like someone riding a blind camel, stumbling erratically. He then addresses a potential objection about whether the obligation to present the definition is rational or conventional.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and its explanation
Read the passage containing the blind camel simile and the commentary that follows.
Pay attention to the metaphor: 'ركب متن العماية'
2
Understand the metaphor
The rider on the blind camel represents someone who proceeds without a proper definition. The 'likely' (awshaka) indicates that the mere conception does not guarantee full benefits.
3
Identify the objection and the answer
The objection states that if beginning with insight is rationally dependent on the six matters, then the obligation is rational. The answer explains that it only necessitates absolute presentation if that insight is rationally obligatory.
The answer uses the concept of 'tawqif' (dependence) and 'taqyid' (restriction).
4
Reflect on the outcome
Consider how this shows that the definitions are presented to ensure rational insight, but the obligation is ultimately conventional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأحق في طرق التعليم ) وفيه مقاصد ) ستة أيضاً ( الأول تعريفه ) أي تعريف العلم الذي يطلب تحصيله وإنما وجب تقديم تعريفه ليكون طالبه على بصيرة ) في طلبه فإنه إذا تصوره بتعريفه سواء كان حداً لمفهوم اسمه، أو رسماً له، فقد أحاط بجميعه إحاطة إجمالية باعتبار أمر شامل له يضبطه ويميزه عما عداه بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره فإنه وإن فرض أنه يكفيه في طلبه لكنه لا يفيده بصيرةً فيه (فإن من ركب متن العماية بمعنى الباطل ( أوشك أن يخبط خبط عشواء ) وهي الناقة التي لا عمياء) وهي قوله : (بمعنى الباطل) وهو هاهنا التصور بغير التعريف من الوجه الأعم، أو الأخص شبهه بالمركوبة في كون كل منهما سبباً لسلوك طريق الرسول إلى المطلوب، وأثبت المتن والركوب ففي الكلام استعارة بالكناية، وتخييل وترشيح وإنما قال : أوشك لأنه بمجرد التصور المذكور لا الشروع مشتملاً على فوائد الأمور الستة فلا شك أن الشروع بمثل هذه البصيرة موقوف عقلاً على الأشياء الستة، فيكون وجوب تقديمها أيضاً عقلياً والجواب أن توقف الشروع بالبصيرة المخصوصة عقلاً على الأمور المذكورة، إنما يوجب تقديمها على الإطلاق أعني ابتداء من غير تقييد بشيء، إذا كان الشروع بتلك البصيرة واجباً عقلياً على الشارع في العلم، من حيث هو طالب وهذا ظاهر على أنه يمكن أن يقال : المراد مطلق الشروع بالبصيرة، والمراد بتوقفه على الأمور المذكورة توقفه على نوعها كما حققناه في حواشي المطول . : له) قوله : ( سواء كان حد المفهوم اسمه أو رسماً (له قال رحمه الله تعالى : لا يخفى عليك أن اسم كل علم موضوع بإزاء مفهوم إجمالي شامل ،له فإن فصل في تعريفه ذلك المفهوم نفسه، كان حداً له بحسب اسمه وإن بين لازمه كان رسماً له بحسب اسمه، وعلى التقديرين هو : رسم لذلك العلم مميز له عن غيره وأما حده الحقيقي فإنما هو بتصور مسائله بل بتصور التصديقات المتعلقة بها، وليس ذلك من مقدمات الشروع . قوله: بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره فإنه وإن فرض أنه يكفيه في طلبه لكنه لا يفيده بصيرةً فيه) أراد بغيره غير التعريف، وغير التعريف يحتمل أن يكون وجهاً أعم، وكونه كافياً في طلب العلم الخاص من حيث خصوصه محل ،تردد فلهذا أورد قوله : وإن فرض إلخ لأن الكلام في الغير المطلق الشامل للاعم، وقوله : لكنه لا يفيد بصيرةً كاملةً يحصل بالتعريف، ثم الكلام في التصورات التي يمكن تقديمها على الشروع كما هو الظاهر من السياق، فلا يرد أن التصور الحدي للعلم باعتبار الحقيقة غير ما ذكر مع أنه على تقدير فرض كفايته في الطلب، ليس مما لا يفيد البصيرة، وذلك لأنه لا يحصل إلا بعد تمام تحصيل العلم المشروع فيه . قوله: (فإن من ركب إلخ هذا في موقع التعليل لإيجاب تصور العلم بتعريفه ليحصل البصيرة، ثم إن انتفاء هذا التصور المخصوص قد يكون بانتفاء أصل التصور، ولظهور عدم إمكان الشروع بدونه لم يتعرض له، وقد يكون بتصور لا يفيد البصيرة المذكورة، كالتصور بوجه أعم، وهو الذي أشار إليه بقوله : فإن من ركب إلخ .
And the most appropriate in the methods of teaching — and in it are also six objectives: the first is its definition, i.e., the definition of the science that one seeks to master. Indeed, it is obligatory to present its definition so that the student has insight into his pursuit. For if he conceives of it through its definition, whether it is a proper definition of the concept of its name or a description of it, he has encompassed all of it with a comprehensive coverage by virtue of a matter that encompasses it, governs it, and distinguishes it from others. Unlike if he conceives of it through something else, for even if it is assumed that it suffices him in his pursuit, it does not give him insight into it. (For whoever rides the mount of 'al-ʿamāyah' meaning falsehood is likely to stumble erratically like a blind camel). That is his statement: 'meaning falsehood,' and here it is the conception without definition from a more general or more specific aspect; he likened it to a mount in that each is a cause for traversing the path to the desired goal. And he affirmed the mount and the riding, so in the expression there is a metaphor by way of implicitness, imagery, and elaboration. And he only said 'likely' because by that conception alone, the beginning comprising the benefits of the six matters does not [result]. So there is no doubt that beginning with such insight is rationally dependent on the six matters; thus, the obligation to present them is also rational. The answer: that the rational dependence of the beginning with the specific insight on the mentioned matters only necessitates their absolute presentation—meaning starting without being restricted by anything—if the beginning with that insight is rationally obligatory on the starter in the science, insofar as he is a seeker. This is apparent, but it can be said that the intended meaning is the absolute beginning with insight, and the intended meaning of its dependence on the mentioned matters is its dependence on their type, as we have verified in the footnotes of al-Muṭawwal. His statement: 'whether it is a proper definition of the concept of its name or a description of it.' He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is not hidden from you that the name of every science is placed in correspondence with a comprehensive concept that encompasses it. So if he specifies in its definition that concept itself, then it is a proper definition with respect to its name; and if he explains its necessary concomitant, then it is a description with respect to its name. In both cases, it is a description of that science, distinguishing it from others. As for its real definition, it is only through conceiving its subject matters, nay, through conceiving the affirmations related to them, and that is not from the preliminaries of beginning. His statement: 'Unlike if he conceives of it through something else, for even if it is assumed that it suffices him in his pursuit, it does not give him insight into it.' By 'something else' he means other than the definition. And 'other than the definition' can be a more general aspect, and its sufficiency for seeking the specific science with respect to its specificity is a matter of doubt. Hence he invoked his statement 'even if it is assumed, etc.' because the discourse concerns the absolute 'other' that includes the more general. And his statement 'but it does not give him complete insight that is obtained by definition' — then the discourse concerns conceptions that can be presented before the beginning, as is apparent from the context. Therefore, it is not objected that the conceptual definition of the science with respect to reality is different from what is mentioned, and that assuming its sufficiency in the pursuit is not something that does not give insight, because it is not obtained except after complete mastery of the science in which one has begun. His statement: 'For whoever rides, etc.' — this is in the position of a cause for the obligation of conceiving the science through its definition in order to obtain insight. Then the absence of this specific conception may be due to the absence of the conception altogether, and due to the obvious impossibility of beginning without it, he did not address it. Or it may be a conception that does not give the mentioned insight, like conception from a more general aspect, and that is what he pointed to by his statement: 'For whoever rides, etc.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This long passage covers the first objective (المقصد الأول) which is the definition of the science (تعريفه). It explains why the definition must be presented: so that the student has insight (بصيرة) into what he is studying. If he conceptualizes the science through its definition (whether a true definition of its name or a description), he grasps it in a comprehensive manner. The passage then gives the simile of a rider on a blind camel (متن العماية) which illustrates the danger of proceeding without proper understanding. It then addresses an objection regarding rational obligation, arguing that the obligation to present these matters is not rational but conventional. Subsequently, the passage elaborates on the nature of definition: the definition can be either a proper definition (حد) of the concept of the name or a description (رسم). The real definition of the science, however, is through its subject matter and propositions, but that is not part of the preliminaries. The passage also discusses the phrase 'بخلاف ما إذا تصوره بغيره' (unlike if he conceptualizes it otherwise), explaining that other conceptions may be too general or too specific and do not provide full insight. Finally, it notes that the loss of proper conceptualization may stem from lacking any conception or from having one that does not yield insight, with the latter being the point of the rider simile.

Key Terms
متن العماية
خبط عشواء
بصيرة
عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the obligation to present definitions is purely a matter of divine command.
Correction: The passage shows that the obligation is based on the need for rational insight, but it is not strictly rational; it is conventional.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of riding a blind camel represent?
A) The necessity of having a teacher
B) The danger of proceeding without a proper definition
C) The importance of memorization
D) The difficulty of kalam

Key Takeaway
Proceeding without a proper definition is like riding a blind camel, and the obligation to present definitions is based on the need for rational insight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dived into learning an Islamic science without first clarifying its definition and scope? What was the result, and how can you prevent that from happening again?
?
The blind camel analogy warns that proceeding without definition leads to stumbling. Where in your life are you currently 'riding blind'—studying or acting without clear understanding—and what one step can you take today to gain true insight?
?
Sanusi shows that even rational obligation is not absolute. Are there areas of your religious practice where you assume something is 'rationally obligatory' when it may actually be conventional? How will you investigate that?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate

Types of Definition: Ḥadd and Rasm
Sanussi details the two kinds of definition: proper definition and description relative to the name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between a proper definition (ḥadd) and a description (rasm) relative to the name of a science, and why the real definition of a science is not part of the preliminaries.

Core Idea
A science can be defined by its name through a ḥadd or a rasm, but its real definition is through its subject matters.

In this passage, Sanusi elaborates on the two types of definition mentioned earlier: the definition can be a proper definition of the concept of the name (ḥadd) or a description (rasm). He clarifies that both serve to distinguish the science, but the real definition of the science is only through its subject matters and is not part of the preliminaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on types of definition
Read the Arabic text that discusses ḥadd and rasm relative to the name, and the real definition.
2
Distinguish between ḥadd and rasm
Ḥadd is when the concept itself is specified; rasm is when a necessary concomitant is explained. Both are definitions relative to the name.
The text says: 'إن فصل في تعريفه ذلك المفهوم نفسه، كان حداً له بحسب اسمه وإن بين لازمه كان رسماً له بحسب اسمه'
3
Understand that both are descriptive
Both a ḥadd and a rasm relative to the name are descriptive of the science and distinguish it from others.
The text says: 'وعلى التقديرين هو : رسم لذلك العلم مميز له عن غيره'
4
Grasp the real definition
The real definition (al-ḥadd al-ḥaqīqī) is through the subject matters and the affirmations related to them, and this is not part of the preliminaries.
The text says: 'وأما حده الحقيقي فإنما هو بتصور مسائله بل بتصور التصديقات المتعلقة بها، وليس ذلك من مقدمات الشروع'

What Is Kalam?

The Definition of Ilm al-Kalam and Its Two Investigations
Provides the definition of kalam and explains its two research points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of ilm al-kalam and the two key investigations that clarify its scope and implications.

Core Idea
Kalam is not just knowing beliefs but being able to prove and defend them.

This passage presents the classical definition of ilm al-kalam as a science that grants a complete and permanent ability to establish religious beliefs on others by proofs and refute doubts. The author then points out two important investigations: one about what 'ilm means in this context, and another about the implications of the words used. Understanding these two points reveals the precise scope of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its parts
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice that the definition has two parts: establishing beliefs and repelling doubts.
The author says the first part indicates the efficient cause, the second the removal of hindrance.
2
Understand the two investigations
The author presents two abhath (investigations). The first questions the meaning of 'ilm, the second analyzes the wording (iqtidar and ma'a). Make sure you see how each expands the definition.
3
Reflect on the definition's implications
Consider why the author emphasizes complete and permanent ability. This shows kalam is not mere knowledge but a practical skill for debate and defense.
The definition distinguishes kalam from logic.

قوله : ( والكلام علم يقتدر (معه ) أي يحصل مع ذلك العلم حصولاً دائمياً عادياً قدرة تامة على إثبات العقائد الدينية على الغير وإلزامه إياها بإيراد الحجج عليها ودفع الشبه عنها فالأول إشارة إلى المقتضي، والثاني إلى انتفاء المانع وهاهنا أبحاث الأول أنه أراد بالعلم معناه الأعم، أو التصديق مطلقا ليتناول إدراك المخطئ في العقائد ودلائلها على ما صرح به الثاني أنه نبه بصيغة الاقتدار على القدرة التامة وبإطلاق المعية على المصاحبة الدائمة فينطبق التعريف على العقائد ما يتوقف عليه إثباتها من ورد الشبه لأن تلك القدرة الأدلة، العلم بجميع على ذلك الإثبات، إنما تصاحب دائماً هذا العلم دون العلم بالقوانين التي يستفاد منها صور الدلائل فقط
His saying: (And kalam is a science by which one is able) i.e., there occurs with that science a permanent customary complete ability to establish religious beliefs upon others and to compel them to them by adducing proofs against them and repelling doubts about them. The first [part] indicates the efficient cause, and the second the removal of the hindrance. And here there are investigations: First, that he intended by 'ilm its general meaning, or absolute tasdiq (assent), so that it may include the perception of the erring one in beliefs and their proofs, as he stated. Second, that he indicated by the form of iqtidar (ability) the complete ability, and by the unrestricted 'ma'a' (with) the permanent accompaniment. So the definition applies to the beliefs and to what their establishment depends on from repelling doubts, because that ability to produce proofs for that establishment accompanies this science always, without the science of the laws from which the forms of proofs are derived only.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of ilm al-kalam and explains its implications. The author states that kalam is a science by which one has a complete and permanent ability to establish religious beliefs upon others and to compel them by adducing proofs and repelling doubts. The first part of the definition (establishing beliefs) indicates the efficient cause, and the second part (repelling doubts) indicates the removal of hindrances. Then two investigations (abhath) follow. The first investigation concerns the meaning of 'ilm: whether the author intended its general meaning or absolute tasdiq (assent), so that the definition includes the perception of the one who errs in beliefs and their proofs (i.e., even false beliefs). The second investigation notes that the form iqtidar (ability) indicates complete ability, and the unrestricted ma'a (with) indicates permanent accompaniment. Therefore, the definition covers both the beliefs themselves and what their establishment depends on, such as repelling doubts. The author specifies that this ability accompanies this science (kalam) always, distinguishing it from the science of logic (the laws from which the forms of proofs are derived), which is only a tool.

Correction: The definition emphasizes a complete and permanent ability to establish beliefs on others by adducing proofs and repelling doubts, highlighting the active, defensive, and demonstrative nature of kalam.

Objection and Answer: Does the Definition Include Divine and Prophetic Knowledge?
Addresses the objection that the definition includes divine and prophetic knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of kalam using 'yaqtadir' excludes non-acquired knowledge such as that of God, the Prophet, and the angels.

Core Idea
The form 'ifti'al' in 'yaqtadir' implies acquisition, excluding unrevealed knowledge.

The definition of kalam as 'knowledge by which one is able to argue' raises an important question: does it include the knowledge of God, the Prophet, and angels? This objection is answered by showing that the word 'yaqtadir' in its Arabic form indicates human acquisition, thus excluding divine and prophetic knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's concern that the definition seems to include divine and prophetic knowledge, which are not called kalam in common usage.
The objector contrasts with the definition from al-Maqasid.
2
Examine the answer's three exclusions
The author answers by explaining three exclusions: the Prophet's knowledge (revelation), angels' knowledge, and God's knowledge. Note the linguistic reasoning using the form of 'yaqtadir'.
3
Understand the linguistic reasoning
Focus on how 'ifti'al' implies endeavor and acquisition, and how the verbal noun indicates temporal origination (huduth). These exclude divine knowledge.
The commentator notes that 'God knows' is metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : المشهور أن علم الله تعالى وعلم الرسول وعلم الملائكة بالاعتقاديات لا يسمى علم الكلام كما أن علمه تعالى بالعمليات، وكذلك علم الرسول وعلم الملائكة بها لا فقهاً، وليس في هذا التعريف ما يخرجها بخلاف التعريف المقاصد، وهو العلم بالعقائد الدينية عن الأدلة اليقينية وادعاء إطلاق علم الكلام المذكور في عليها بعيد من المتعارف، قلت : يمكن أن يخرج علم الرسول عليه السلام وعلم الملائكة بكلمة يقتدر بناء على أن صيغة الافتعال تدل على الاعتمال المشعر بالكسب وعلمه عليه السلام بالكشف المسمى بالوحي وكذا علم الملائكة وأما علم الله تعالى فيخرج بها أيضا بذلك الاعتبار، وباعتبار دلالة لفظ الفعل على الحدوث، وأما علم الله تعالى ويعلم الله فمن قبيل المجاز كما صرح به الشارح في حواشيه على المطول .
For if you say: It is well-known that the knowledge of God Most High, and the knowledge of the Messenger, and the knowledge of the angels concerning beliefs is not called 'ilm al-kalam, just as His knowledge of practical matters, and likewise the knowledge of the Messenger and the knowledge of the angels concerning them is not fiqh. And there is nothing in this definition that excludes them, unlike the definition of al-Maqasid, which is knowledge of religious beliefs from definitive proofs. And claiming that the term 'ilm al-kalam is applied to them is far from common usage. He said: I say: It is possible to exclude the knowledge of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and the knowledge of the angels by the word 'yaqtadir' (is able), based on the fact that the form of ifti'al (iqtidar) indicates endeavor implying acquisition. And his knowledge, peace be upon him, is by uncovering called revelation, and likewise the knowledge of the angels. As for the knowledge of God Most High, it is excluded by it as well by that consideration, and by the consideration of the indication of the verbal noun on temporal origination. And as for the knowledge of God Most High, and God knows, it is of the category of metaphor, as stated by the commentator in his footnotes on al-Mutawwal.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the definition of kalam. The objector points out that the knowledge of God, the Prophet, and the angels concerning beliefs is not called 'ilm al-kalam in common usage, yet the definition provided (علم يقتدر معه) does not seem to exclude them. This contrasts with another definition (that of al-Maqasid) which explicitly restricts to knowledge derived from definitive proofs. The answer is that the word 'yaqtadir' (is able) in the definition, which is in the form of ifti'al, implies acquisition (kash), and thus excludes knowledge that is not acquired, such as the Prophet's knowledge by revelation or the angels' knowledge. Divine knowledge is excluded because it is not acquired and also because the verbal noun form indicates temporal origination (huduth), which does not apply to God. The commentator notes that expressions like 'God knows' are metaphorical.

علم الملائكة

اكتساب
وحي
حدوث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of kalam incorrectly includes divine and prophetic knowledge.
Correction: The word 'yaqtadir' (form ifti'al) implies acquisition, so it excludes knowledge acquired without effort (revelation) and divine knowledge (eternal, not temporal).

Definition of Kalam and Its Two Parts
Defines 'ilm al-kalam and explains its two parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'ilm al-kalam and its two essential components: presenting proofs and repelling doubts.

Core Idea
Kalam is the science that equips you to prove Islam and refute doubts.

This lesson presents the classical definition of 'ilm al-kalam as formulated by Imam al-Iji and explained by al-Jurjani. The definition highlights both the positive and negative aspects of theological reasoning: establishing religious doctrines and removing obstacles to their acceptance. By understanding these two parts, you grasp the full scope of kalam's purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the definition.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two parts
The definition mentions two actions: 'إيراد الحجج' (presenting proofs) and 'دفع الشبه' (repelling doubts). Note that the commentary interprets these as the 'requisite' and 'removal of obstacle'.
These two parts are the positive and negative aspects of kalam.
3
Reflect on why both are necessary
Consider why a science of theology would need both to prove truth and refute falsehood. How do these two parts complement each other?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام علم بأمور يقتدر معه أي يحصل مع ذلك العلم حصولاً دائمياً عادياً قدرة تامة على إثبات العقائد الدينية على الغير وإلزامه إياها بإيراد الحجج عليها ودفع الشبه عنها فالأول إشارة إلى المقتضي، والثاني إلى انتفاء المانع
And al-kalam is a science of matters by which one is able, i.e., there occurs with that knowledge a continuous, habitual occurrence, a complete ability to establish religious doctrines upon others and to compel them to accept them by presenting proofs for them and repelling doubts against them. So the first part refers to the requisite, and the second to the removal of the obstacle.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines the science of kalam and explains its two essential components. The definition includes the idea of ability (iqtidar) that comes with this knowledge, leading to a complete capacity to prove religious beliefs to others. The two parts of the definition are then interpreted: 'presenting proofs' indicates the positive requisite for establishing doctrines, while 'repelling doubts' indicates the removal of obstacles that prevent acceptance.

دفع الشبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The definition explicitly includes both presenting proofs and repelling doubts, which removes obstacles to acceptance.

B) إيراد الحجج ودفع الشبه

D) اليقين والشك

Key Takeaway
Kalam is defined by both positively proving religious doctrines and negatively removing doubts.

The Meaning of 'Ilm in the Definition of Kalam
Examines the intended meaning of 'ilm in the definition of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author interprets 'ilm in the definition of kalam in a general sense to include the perception of the mistaken person.

Core Idea
'Ilm in kalam's definition includes even mistaken understanding.

This passage examines a key ambiguity in the definition of kalam: should 'ilm (knowledge) be understood strictly or broadly? The author argues for a broad interpretation—including even mistaken beliefs—so that the definition covers all attempts at theological argument, correct or not. We will explore the reasoning behind this choice and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'ليتناول إدراك المخطئ' which is central.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'ilm
The passage presents two possible meanings: the general sense (absolute assent) and the strict sense (an attribute that does not admit contradiction). Find where each is mentioned and note how they differ.
The strict sense will be discussed later in the work.
3
Understand why the general sense is chosen
Ask yourself why the author opts for the general sense. The commentary explains that this is a metaphorical usage to include the mistaken person. Reflect on why the strict sense would exclude such perception.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول أنه أراد بالعلم معناه الأعم، أو التصديق مطلقا ليتناول إدراك المخطئ في العقائد ودلائلها على ما صرح به قوله : ( أراد بالعلم معناه الأعم أو التصديق مطلقاً كأنه حمل العلم على المعنى المجازي بقرينة المقام، وإلا فسيصرح في تزييف تفسير العلم بالمعنى الأعم، أن إطلاق العلم على الجهل المركب يخالف استعمال الدنة[؟] والعرف والشرع ولا يمكن حمل العلم هاهنا على ما سيأتي من الصفة الموجبة للتميز الغير المحتمل للنقيض، لأن المراد هناك عدم الاحتمال بوجه من الوجوه لا أعم مما في نفس الأمر، وعند من قامت به فيخرج إدراك المخطئ قطعاً فليتأمل.
The first is that he intended by 'ilm its general meaning, or absolute assent, so that it includes the perception of the mistaken person in the doctrines and their proofs, as he stated. He said: (He intended by 'ilm its general meaning or absolute assent) as if he took 'ilm in a metaphorical sense due to the context, otherwise he will clearly state in refuting the interpretation of 'ilm in the general sense that applying 'ilm to compound ignorance contradicts usage, custom, and law, and it is not possible to carry 'ilm here to what will come later of the attribute that necessitates distinction that does not admit of contradiction, because what is intended there is absolute non-contradiction in every respect, not a general [non-contradiction] relative to the actual state, and for one in whom it subsists, the perception of the mistaken person is definitely excluded, so reflect.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an investigation into the meaning of 'ilm in the definition of kalam. The author argues that 'ilm is used in a general sense or as absolute assent to include even the mistaken understanding of doctrines, so that the definition covers all attempts at argumentation, whether correct or mistaken. The commentary then explains that this is a metaphorical usage, as applying 'ilm to compound ignorance would violate linguistic and legal norms. The true definition of 'ilm as a distinguishing attribute that does not admit of contradiction would exclude the mistaken person's perception, so careful reflection is needed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'ilm in any context must always mean true knowledge that cannot be mistaken.
Correction: In this specific definition of kalam, 'ilm is used metaphorically to include even mistaken beliefs, so that the definition covers all attempts at argumentation, correct or not.

Key Takeaway
In the definition of kalam, 'ilm is understood broadly to include both correct and mistaken perceptions, ensuring the definition covers all theological argumentation.

The Import of 'Yuqtadir' and Defending the Definition
Explains the import of 'yuqtadir' and defends the definition against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'yuqtadir' in the definition of kalam implies constant ability and acquisition, and how the definition excludes the knowledge of God, prophets, and angels.

Core Idea
The form ifti'al in 'yuqtadir' signals acquisition, excluding revealed knowledge from kalam.

This passage delves into the second investigation of the definition of kalam. It explains why the form 'yuqtadir' is used and addresses an objection: if the definition includes God's, prophets', and angels' knowledge, why isn't that called kalam? The answer hinges on the distinction between acquired and revealed knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the form 'yuqtadir'
Notice that the form ifti'al indicates complete ability and constant companionship, which applies to the science of kalam.
This is the basis for the definition applying to doctrines and their proofs.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the knowledge of God, the Prophet, and the angels about beliefs is well-known not to be called kalam, yet the definition does not exclude them.
This objection questions the definition's scope.
3
Follow the answer
The answer uses the form ifti'al to imply acquisition (kasb), and distinguishes acquired knowledge from revealed knowledge (wahy). God's knowledge is also excluded by temporality or metaphor.
Thus the definition remains valid only for human acquired knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أنه نبه بصيغة الاقتدار على القدرة التامة وبإطلاق المعية على المصاحبة الدائمة فينطبق التعريف على العقائد ما يتوقف عليه إثباتها من ورد الشبه لأن تلك القدرة الأدلة، العلم بجميع على ذلك الإثبات، إنما تصاحب دائماً هذا العلم دون العلم بالقوانين التي يستفاد منها فإن قلت: المشهور أن علم الله تعالى وعلم الرسول وعلم الملائكة بالاعتقاديات لا يسمى علم الكلام كما أن علمه تعالى بالعمليات، وكذلك علم الرسول وعلم الملائكة بها لا فقهاً، وليس في هذا التعريف ما يخرجها بخلاف التعريف المذكور في المقاصد، وهو العلم بالعقائد الدينية عن الأدلة اليقينية وادعاء إطلاق علم الكلام عليها بعيد من المتعارف، قلت: يمكن أن يخرج علم الرسول عليه السلام وعلم الملائكة بكلمة يقتدر بناء على أن صيغة الافتعال تدل على الاعتمال المشعر بالكسب وعلمه عليه السلام بالكشف المسمى بالوحي وكذا علم الملائكة وأما علم الله تعالى فيخرج بها أيضا بذلك الاعتبار، وباعتبار دلالة لفظ الفعل على الحدوث، وأما علم الله تعالى ويعلم الله فمن قبيل المجاز كما صرح به الشارح في حواشيه على المطول.
The second is that he alerted by the form of iqtidar to complete ability and by the absoluteness of accompaniment to constant companionship, so the definition applies to the doctrines and what their proof depends on from the repulsion of doubts, because that ability and knowledge of all the proofs for that establishment only constantly accompany this science, not the science of the laws from which the forms of proofs are derived. If you say: The well-known fact is that the knowledge of God, the knowledge of the Prophet, and the knowledge of the angels concerning beliefs is not called 'ilm al-kalam, just as His knowledge of practical matters, and likewise the knowledge of the Prophet and the angels regarding them is not called fiqh, and this definition does not exclude them, unlike the definition in al-Maqasid, which is knowledge of religious doctrines from certain proofs, and claiming that the term kalam is applied to them is far from common usage. I say: It is possible to exclude the knowledge of the Prophet and the angels by the word yaftadir (being able) because the form ifti'al indicates exertion that implies acquisition, while his knowledge is by unveiling called revelation, and similarly the knowledge of the angels. As for the knowledge of God, it is also excluded by that consideration, and by the consideration that the verbal noun indicates temporality. As for the knowledge of God and 'He knows God' are metaphorical, as the commentator stated in his glosses on al-Mutawwal.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an investigation into the significance of the phrase 'yuqtadir ma'ahu' in the definition. The author argues that the form ifti'al indicates a constant ability. Then an objection is raised: the knowledge of God, prophets, and angels about beliefs is not called kalam, so the definition should exclude them. The answer distinguishes between acquired knowledge (kasbi) and revealed knowledge (wahy), and also notes that God's knowledge is not subject to temporality.

Kalam's Distinctiveness from Dialectic and Grammar
Distinguishes kalam from dialectic and grammar, emphasizing complete ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam is distinct from dialectic and grammar and why they are not included in its definition.

Core Idea
Kalam alone provides complete ability to establish religious doctrines.

Here the author clarifies what kalam is not. He distinguishes it from dialectic (jadal) and grammar (nahw), showing that they lack the complete ability (iqtidār tāmm) required for establishing Islamic doctrines. This helps refine the definition of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about dialectic and grammar
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the author contrasts kalam with each science.
2
Identify the reasons for exclusion
Find why dialectic is excluded: it lacks complete ability and is not specific to these doctrines. Then find why grammar is excluded: it does not guarantee that ability in all cases.
The phrase 'اقتدار تام' is key.
3
Understand the meaning of 'complete ability'
Reflect on what 'complete ability' means: the power to establish doctrines from their proofs and repel doubts at will. Dialectic and grammar fall short.
This concept is central to the definition of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور الدلائل فقط، ودون علم الجدل الذي يتوسل به إلى حفظ أي وضع يراد، إذ ليس فيه اقتدار تمام على ذلك، وإن سلم فلا اختصاص له بإثبات هذه العقائد والمتبادر هذا الحد: ما له نوع اختصاص به، ودون علم النحو المجامع لعلم الكلام مثلاً، إذ ليس يترتب عليه تلك القدرة دائما على جميع التقادير بل لا مدخل له في ذلك الترتب العادي أصلاً ، الثالث أنه اختار يقتدر على يثبت لأن الإثبات بالفعل غير لازم، واختار من المنطق يستفاد منه مناسبة المبادي أيضاً، وهي الصحة من حيث المادة لأن أكثر نظر المنطق في صحة الصورة . قوله : (إذ ليس فيه اقتدار تام لأن الاقتدار التام على ذلك الإثبات إنما يحصل بعد حصول العقائد المذكورة عن أدلتها، ودفع الشبهة عنها بالفعل والتمكن من استحضارها متى شاء، وأما علم الجدل والمنطق فإنما يفيد أن التمكن على ذلك الإثبات في الجملة، بمعنى أنه إذا حصل مباديها ورتبها أمكن له ذلك الإثبات . قوله : ( وإن العقائد) إلخ يريد أنه لو قال : يقتدر معه على تحصيل العقائد الدينية بإيراد الحجج، لتوهم منه أن يراد الحجج ولو عقلية، ودفع الشبه كاف في تحصيل العقائد، وليس كذلك بل لا بد من الأخذ من الشرع فأشار بذكر الإثبات إلى أن ثمرته الإثبات لا التحصيل، لكن لا يخفى أن الإشعار خفي لأن ذكر الإثبات لا يدل على نفي التحصيل حتى يشعر بأن تحصل من المنطق، ومادة معينة لا تعرف منه فالحصر المستفاد من قوله : فقط بالنظر إلى المواد المخصوصة، والعقائد معاً على ما هو التحقيق، وبهذا تبين ضعف ما استصوبه الشارح في حواشي شرح المطالع من أن الطرق والشرائط المحتاج إليها في استحصال المطالب لو كانت ضرورية لم يقع الغلط لا من جهة الصورة، وهو ظاهر ولا من جهة المادة لأن تلك الطرق والشرائط تراعي جانب المادة رعايتها جانب الصورة ووجه الضعف أن خصوصيات المواد لا تعلم من المنطق، وإنما المستفاد منه معرفة مناسبة المبادئ المعلومة من علوم آخر بالنسبة إلى كل مطلوب على وجه إجمالي، فمع ضرورية جميع قواعد المنطق يجوز الغلط من جهة المادة قطعاً . قوله : (بل لا مدخل له في ذلك الترتيب العادي أصلا فلا يدخل في التعريف المجموع المركب من علم الكلام وغيره أيضاً، فإن المتبادر منه اعتبار المدخلية قطعاً، وأيضاً المجموع ليس علماً واحداً بل علمين أو علوماً جمةً، وبهذا يظهر خروج المجموع المركب من علمي الكلام والجدل، وكذا المركب من علمي الكلام والتفسير لا بانتفاء مدخلية علم التفسير الترتيب العادي المذكور ، لأن دخله لا يبعد عن دخل نفس العقائد بل ربما يدعى أن النحو أيضا له مدخل في ذلك الترتيب لأن بعض العقائد مستفاد من الأدلة السمعية، فيكون لعلم النحو مدخل في القدرة على إثبات تلك العقائد المستفادة منها، وإن لم يتوقف كما في أرباب السليقة المستنبطين لتلك العقائد منها .
Only the forms of proofs, and not the science of dialectic which is used to preserve any position intended, for there is not complete ability in it for that, and even if conceded, it is not specific to establishing these doctrines, and the apparent meaning of this definition is that it has a kind of specificity to it. And not the science of grammar which is combined with kalam, for example, for that ability does not follow from it always in all cases, rather it has no entry in that habitual arrangement at all. The third is that he chose yuqtadir over yuthbit because actual establishment is not necessary, and he chose from logic that the suitability of premises is also derived from it, which is truth in terms of matter, because most of logic's concern is the validity of form. He said: (For there is not complete ability in it) because complete ability for that establishment only occurs after obtaining the mentioned doctrines from their proofs and repelling the doubt about them actually and being able to recall them whenever he wishes. As for the science of dialectic and logic, they only provide the ability for that establishment in general, meaning that if one obtains its premises and arranges them, he can establish. He said: (And the doctrines) etc., meaning that if he said 'he is able with it to acquire the religious doctrines by presenting proofs', it might be imagined that what is meant is proofs even if rational, and repelling doubts is sufficient for acquiring doctrines, but it is not so; rather, it is necessary to take from revelation. So he indicated by mentioning 'establishment' that its fruit is establishment, not acquisition. However, it is not hidden that the suggestion is subtle because mentioning establishment does not indicate negation of acquisition such that it suggests that acquisition comes from logic, and a specific matter is not known from it. So the restriction derived from his saying 'only' considers the specific matters and the doctrines together, according to verification. By this, the weakness of what the commentator approved in his glosses on Sharh al-Mutalla' is shown, namely that if the methods and conditions needed for acquiring the required matters were necessary, error would not occur either in terms of form—which is apparent—nor in terms of matter, because those methods and conditions observe the side of matter as much as they observe the side of form. The weakness is that the particularities of matters are not known from logic; rather, what is derived from it is knowledge of the suitability of premises known from other sciences for each required matter in a general way. So with the necessity of all logical rules, error is definitely possible from the side of matter. He said: (Rather, it has no entry in that habitual arrangement at all) so it does not enter the definition of the composite whole of kalam and other sciences, for the apparent meaning considers entry definitely. Also, the whole is not one science but two or many sciences. By this, the exclusion of the composite of kalam and dialectic is shown, and likewise the composite of kalam and tafsir, not because the entry of tafsir is not in that habitual arrangement, because its entry is not far from the entry of the doctrines themselves. Rather, it might be claimed that grammar also has an entry in that arrangement because some doctrines are derived from scriptural proofs, so grammar has an entry in the ability to establish those doctrines derived from them, even if it is not necessary, as in the case of those with natural aptitude who derive those doctrines from them.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage distinguishes kalam from logic, dialectic, and grammar, and presents the third investigation. It argues that logic alone does not provide complete ability to establish doctrines because it only gives the form of proofs, not the specific material content. Dialectic and grammar are also insufficient. The third investigation discusses the choice of 'yuqtadir' over 'yuthbit'. The passage then refutes a view that logical rules prevent error in both form and matter, arguing that material errors are still possible because logic does not teach the specific premises.

علم الجدل
علم النحو
الاقتدار التام
الإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dialectic or grammar can provide the same ability as kalam to establish religious doctrines.
Correction: The author shows they lack complete ability (iqtidār tāmm) and are not specific to these doctrines.

Checkpoint
Why is grammar excluded from the definition of kalam?
A) Because grammar is not a science
B) Because grammar does not always provide the ability to establish doctrines
C) Because grammar is concerned with forms of proofs
D) Because grammar is specific to Arabic

Key Takeaway
Kalam is uniquely qualified to establish religious doctrines with complete ability, unlike dialectic or grammar.

?

?
This passage excludes grammar from playing any role in establishing doctrines. In what areas of your faith have you mistakenly relied on language or culture alone, rather than on proof-based reasoning? What will you do to shift your reliance?

The Third Investigation: Choosing 'Yuqtadir' and Refuting Logical Infallibility
Explains the third investigation and refutes the view that logic prevents all error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author chose 'yuqtadir' over 'yuthbit' and why logical rules do not prevent all errors in kalam.

Core Idea
Complete establishment requires more than logic; it needs revealed premises.

This lesson examines a subtle point in the definition of kalam. The author explains why he used 'yuqtadir' (he is able) instead of 'yuthbit' (he establishes), and then refutes a claim that logical rules prevent all error, showing that material errors remain possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third investigation and refutation
Read the entire passage once. Pay attention to the arguments about 'yuqtadir' and the critique of the commentator's view.
2
Understand the choice of wording
Identify why the author prefers 'yuqtadir' over 'yuthbit': actual establishment is not always present; the definition should indicate ability. Also note the distinction between establishment (ithbat) and acquisition (tahsil).
The passage mentions that 'ithbat' is the fruit of kalam, not 'tahsil'.
3
Analyze the refutation of logical infallibility
Find the argument against the view that logical rules prevent all error. The author states that material errors are still possible because logic does not teach the specific premises (material).
Key phrase: 'خصوصيات المواد لا تعلم من المنطق'.

Why Logic and Dialectic Are Not Part of Kalam's Definition
Explains why logic and dialectic are insufficient for defining ilm al-kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the science of kalam is defined in a way that excludes logic, dialectic, and grammar, and how the definition preserves specialization and inferential power.

Core Idea
Defining kalam requires excluding sciences that lack specialization in proving religious beliefs.

Scholars of kalam carefully defined their discipline to distinguish it from others. Logic and dialectic might seem helpful, but they lack the specific connection to Islamic doctrines. This passage explains why such sciences are excluded from the definition and what makes kalam unique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the three excluded sciences
Notice that the author excludes logic (forms of proofs), dialectic, and grammar. For each, note the reason given.
The passage mentions 'thirdly' indicating an enumeration.
3
Understand the requirement of specialization
The author insists that the definition must pick out a science that has a kind of specialization (اختصاص) in proving these beliefs. This is key.
4
Reflect on the role of logic
The author limits logic to checking formal validity (soundness of form), not material truth. This is why logic is insufficient.
This connects to the misconception that logic alone can prove beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور الدلائل فقط، ودون علم الجدل الذي يتوسل به إلى حفظ أي وضع يراد، إذ ليس فيه اقتدار تمام على ذلك، وإن سلم فلا اختصاص له بإثبات هذه العقائد والمتبادر هذا الحد: ما له نوع اختصاص به، ودون علم النحو المجامع لعلم الكلام مثلاً، إذ ليس يترتب عليه تلك القدرة دائما على جميع التقادير بل لا مدخل له في ذلك الترتب العادي أصلاً ، الثالث أنه اختار يقتدر على يثبت لأن الإثبات بالفعل غير لازم، واختار من المنطق يستفاد منه مناسبة المبادي أيضاً، وهي الصحة من حيث المادة لأن أكثر نظر المنطق في صحة الصورة .
[It includes] only forms of proofs, and without the science of dialectic which is used to defend any position one wishes, since there is not complete power over that; and even if granted, it has no specialization in proving these beliefs. What is understood from this definition is: that which has a kind of specialization in it. And without the science of grammar, which combines with the science of kalam for example, since that power does not always follow from it in all respects; rather, it has no entry at all in that customary succession. Thirdly, he chose 'he is able to prove' because actual proof is not necessary, and he chose from logic benefit of suitability of premises also, which is soundness in terms of material, because most of logic's consideration is the soundness of form.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage defends a particular definition of ilm al-kalam by excluding sciences that do not have a unique connection to proving religious beliefs. The author argues that the definition should not include 'only forms of proofs' (logic) nor dialectic, because dialectic can be used to defend any position and does not specifically prove these beliefs. Even if it could, it lacks complete power. Grammar is also excluded because, though it might assist in understanding scriptural arguments, its power is not always operative. The author then notes that the choice of the phrase 'he is able to prove' (rather than 'he acquires') indicates that actual proof is not necessary, and logic is useful only for the suitability of premises (material soundness), not for the material itself, since logic mainly checks formal validity.

Key Terms
علم الجدل
اقتدار
اختصاص
المادة
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think logic is sufficient for proving beliefs because it ensures valid arguments.
Correction: Logic only checks formal soundness, not material truth. The material (premises) must be true, which logic does not guarantee.

Checkpoint
Why does the author exclude the science of dialectic from the definition of kalam?
A) Because it is not a science at all.
B) Because it can defend any position and is not specific to proving religious beliefs.
C) Because it focuses only on material truth.
D) Because it is too difficult to learn.

Key Takeaway
The definition of kalam must be specific to sciences that have a unique specialization in proving Islamic beliefs, excluding logic, dialectic, and grammar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your faith, do you ever rely on argumentative techniques that could be used to justify any position, rather than on the specific proofs from revelation and reason that make Islam unique? How will you shift your approach to ensure you are not just using dialectic?
?
Have you ever assumed that a logically sound argument (sound form) automatically proves a belief true, forgetting that the premises themselves must be materially sound? What steps will you take to verify the truth of your premises before engaging in debate?
?
Consider an area of your belief where you feel confident in your arguments. Is that confidence rooted in a specialized understanding of the proofs for that belief, or in general reasoning skills that could be applied to any worldview? What will you do to deepen your specialization in the proofs of kalam?

The Limits of Logic and Dialectic in Kalam
Explains why complete power to prove beliefs requires more than logic and dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why complete power to prove religious beliefs requires more than logic and dialectic alone.

Core Idea
Logic provides the tool; actual beliefs and doubt-refutation provide the power.

In this passage, the commentator explains a crucial point from the definition of kalam: having the ability to prove beliefs is not the same as actually having proven them. Logic and dialectic give only potential, not actual power. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

3

They give ability in general—if you have premises and arrange them, proof is possible. But it doesn't guarantee actual belief possession or doubt refutation.
This is the key limitation.

His saying: 'since there is not in it complete power' because complete power over that proof is only obtained after the aforementioned beliefs are obtained from their proofs, and the refutation of doubts about them actually, and the ability to recall them whenever one wishes. As for the sciences of dialectic and logic, they only give ability over that proof in general, meaning that if one obtains their premises and arranges them, that proof becomes possible for him.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'since there is not in it complete power' from the definition. The author explains that complete power to prove religious beliefs requires actual possession of those beliefs from their (scriptural) proofs, actual refutation of doubts, and the ability to recall them at will. Dialectic and logic only give a general ability: if one has the premises and arranges them correctly, one can produce a proof. But this does not guarantee that one has already obtained the beliefs or can refute all doubts. Thus, these sciences alone are insufficient for the complete power that the definition requires.

Why 'Ithbat' is Preferred Over 'Tahsil' in Defining Kalam
Argues for using 'Ithbat' over 'tahsil' in the definition of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author uses 'Ithbat' instead of 'tahsil' and how this choice reflects kalam's purpose and the necessity of relying on shari'a.

Core Idea
Kalam's fruit is proving beliefs, not merely acquiring them.

In defining kalam, the author deliberately chooses the term 'Ithbat' (proof) over 'tahsil' (acquisition). This choice carries deep implications: it emphasizes that kalam's goal is to demonstrate religious beliefs to others, not just to intellectually apprehend them. Moreover, it guards against the misconception that rational arguments alone can produce correct beliefs without guidance from revelation. In this passage, the author explains why 'Ithbat' is correct and refutes an alternative interpretation by al-Taftazani.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to grasp the author's argument in his own words.
Pay attention to the subtlety mentioned.
2
Identify the two main sections
Notice that the passage first explains why 'Ithbat' is preferable, then refutes al-Taftazani's view that 'Ithbat' could mean acquisition.
3
Analyze the refutation structure
See how the author responds to Taftazani by considering different interpretations of 'science' (ilm) in the definition.
This shows the depth of kalām methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن العقائد) إلخ يريد أنه لو قال : يقتدر معه على تحصيل العقائد الدينية بإيراد الحجج، لتوهم منه أن يراد الحجج ولو عقلية، ودفع الشبه كاف في تحصيل العقائد، وليس كذلك بل لا بد من الأخذ من الشرع فأشار بذكر الإثبات إلى أن ثمرته الإثبات لا التحصيل، لكن لا يخفى أن الإشعار خفي لأن ذكر الإثبات لا يدل على نفي التحصيل حتى يشعر بأن ثمرته التحصيل يجب أن يكون من الشرع غاية ما يقال : كان الظاهر ذكر التحصيل لأن إيراد الحجج، ودفع الشبه علة لحصول العلم بها، فالعدول إلى الإثبات يشعر بنفي كون ثمرته التحصيل. قوله : ( ولا يجوز إلخ رد على العلامة التفتازاني حيث جوز حمل الإثبات على التحصيل وقال : معنى إثبات العقائد الدينية تحصيلها واكتسابها بحيث يحصل الترقي من التقليد أي التحقيق، ووجه دفعه أن ذلك إنما يرد لو حمل العلم على التصديقات، وكذا ملكة الاستحضار فإنها تحصل بعد العلم وتكرار المشاهدة والعلامة التفتازاني، ولو حمل على المسائل المدللة فلا شك في كون التحصيل المذكور ثمرة لها بمعنى أن من طالع تلك المسائل ووقف على أدلتها، حصل له العلم بالعقائد وعلى تقدير حمله على التصديقات، فالعلم هو التصديقات مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية المحل على ما تقرر والثمرة هي التصديقات الجزئية القائمة بالمحل على ما يشعر به لفظ العقائد، وحمله على ملكة الاستحصال كما في شرح المقاصد، يعني التهيؤ القريب بسبب
His saying: 'and that the beliefs (must be taken from shari'a)' – he means that if he had said: 'by which one can acquire the religious beliefs through presenting proofs', it might be misunderstood that proofs, even rational ones, and refuting doubts are sufficient for acquiring beliefs, but that is not the case; rather, one must take from shari'a. So by mentioning 'proof' (Ithbat), he indicates that its fruit is proof, not acquisition. However, it is not hidden that the indication is subtle because mentioning 'proof' does not negate acquisition, such that it indicates that its fruit is acquisition must be from shari'a. At most it is said: the apparent would be mentioning 'acquisition' because presenting proofs and refuting doubts are causes for obtaining knowledge of them, so the deviation to 'proof' indicates negation of its fruit being acquisition. His saying: 'and it is not permissible...' is a refutation of Taftazani, who permitted interpreting 'Ithbat' as acquisition and said: the meaning of 'proving the religious beliefs' is acquiring them and obtaining them such that one rises from taqlid to verification. The refutation is that this would only apply if 'science' is interpreted as the tasdiqat (assents), and similarly the disposition of recall (malakat al-istihdar) is obtained after knowledge and repeated observation. And Taftazani, if he interprets it as the demonstrable issues, then there is no doubt that acquisition is their fruit in the sense that whoever studies those issues and understands their proofs obtains knowledge of the beliefs. And on the assumption that it is interpreted as the tasdiqat, then 'science' is the tasdiqat regardless of the particular subject, and the fruit is the particular tasdiqat pertaining to the subject, as the word 'beliefs' indicates. And interpreting it as the disposition of acquisition (malakat al-istihsal) as in Sharh al-Maqasid, meaning the proximate readiness due to...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage addresses two related points about the definition. First, the author explains why the phrase 'Ithbat al-aqa'id' (proving beliefs) is used instead of 'tahsil' (acquisition). If 'tahsil' were used, one might think that rational proofs alone could acquire beliefs, but actually beliefs must be taken from shari'a. The mention of 'Ithbat' indicates that the fruit of kalam is proving to others. However, the indication is subtle because 'Ithbat' does not explicitly negate acquisition. The author notes that the apparent choice would be 'tahsil' since proofs cause knowledge, so the deviation to 'Ithbat' suggests negation of acquisition as the fruit. Second, the author refutes Taftazani's interpretation that 'Ithbat' can mean acquisition. The refutation considers different interpretations of 'science' (ilm) in the definition: if it means the assents (tasdiqat), then acquiring beliefs is the fruit; if it means the demonstrable issues, then studying them gives knowledge; if it means the disposition of acquisition (malakat al-istihsal), then the definition works without contradiction. The passage ends mid-sentence explaining the latter interpretation.

Correction: The author argues that 'Ithbat' means proving to others, and that beliefs must be derived from shari'a, not just rational proofs.

D) Because al-Taftazani opposed it.

Key Takeaway
The choice of 'Ithbat' over 'tahsil' underscores that kalam's purpose is to prove religious beliefs using shari'a-derived evidence, not merely to acquire them through reason.

The Wording of Kalam's Definition: Customary Causality and Establishment
Explains the rationale behind the wording of kalam's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of ilm al-kalam uses the preposition 'b' to indicate customary rather than real causality, and why 'establishment' (ithbat) was chosen over 'acquisition' (tahsil).

Core Idea
The wording of kalam's definition reveals its purpose: establishing beliefs, not acquiring them.

This passage dives into the precise wording of the definition of ilm al-kalam. The author explains two key choices: the use of the preposition 'b' to signal that the causality is customary, not real, and the preference for 'establishment' over 'acquisition' to highlight that kalam's fruit is convincing others. Understanding these nuances deepens our appreciation of how the science is framed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to understand the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the words 'باء' and 'إثبات' as they are the focus.
2
Identify the two linguistic choices
The passage explains two choices: 1) use of the preposition 'b' despite its commonness, and 2) choice of 'ithbat' over 'tahsil'. Mark these in the text.
3
Understand the first choice: 'b' indicating customary causality
The author says the preposition 'b' is used to remind that real causality is negated, meaning the proofs do not necessarily cause belief. Instead, it indicates customary causality (constant accompaniment).
This guards against the misconception that proofs compel belief.
4
Understand the second choice: 'ithbat' over 'tahsil'
The author chose 'establishment' to show that the fruit of kalam is establishing beliefs for others, not acquiring them oneself. The objection that 'tahsil' would be more obvious is answered: the shift indicates that acquisition is not the fruit.
This highlights that kalam's purpose is apologetic, not personal discovery.
5
Consider the objection and response
Read the objection: why not use 'tahsil' since proofs cause attainment? The response: 'ithbat' negates that acquisition is the fruit; beliefs must come from Shari'a, even if intellect can grasp them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على به مع شيوع استعماله تنبيهاً على انتفاء السببية الحقيقية المتبادرة من الباء هاهنا، واختار إثبات العقائد على تحصيلها إشعاراً بأن ثمرة الكلام إثباتها على الغير وأن العقائد يجب أن تؤخذ من الشرع ليعتد بها، وإن كانت مما يستقل العقل فيه، ولا يجوز حمل الإثبات ها هنا على التحصيل والاكتساب إذ يلزم منه أن يكون العلم التحصيل يجب أن يكون من الشرع غاية ما يقال : كان الظاهر ذكر التحصيل لأن إيراد الحجج، ودفع الشبه علة لحصول العلم بها، فالعدول إلى الإثبات يشعر بنفي كون ثمرته التحصيل. قوله : ( على انتفاء السببية (الحقيقية تقييد السببية بالحقيقة في الانتفاء مشعر بعدم انتفاء السببية ،العادية وهذا لا ينافي المصاحبة الدائمة المرادة هاهنا لما يشير إليه في المقصد الرابع في كيفية إفادة النظر الصحيح للعلم من أن الدوام لا ينافي العادية .
Upon it, despite its widespread usage, as a reminder that real causality is negated from the preposition 'ba' here. And he chose 'establishment' (ithbat) of beliefs over their 'acquisition' (tahsil) to indicate that the fruit of Kalam is their establishment for others, and that beliefs must be taken from the Shari'a to be valid, even if they are among things in which the intellect is independent. And it is not permissible to interpret 'establishment' here as acquisition and earning, because it would necessarily imply that the science of acquisition must be from the Shari'a. At most it can be said: apparently 'acquisition' should have been mentioned because the presentation of proofs and repelling of doubts are causes for the attainment of knowledge of them, so the shift to 'establishment' indicates the negation of acquisition being its fruit. His statement: (On the negation of causality) restricting causality by 'real' in the negation indicates that customary causality is not negated. And this does not contradict the constant accompaniment intended here, as he points out in the fourth goal (al-maqsad al-rabiʿ) about how valid reasoning (nazar) yields knowledge, that constancy does not contradict custom.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the wording of the definition of ilm al-kalam, focusing on the phrase 'بِإِيرَادِ الْحُجَجِ' (by presenting proofs). The author notes that the preposition 'ب' (bi) is used despite its commonness as a reminder that the causality implied by 'ب' is not real causality (sababiyya haqiqiyya), which would mean the proofs themselves necessarily produce belief. Rather, the intended causality is customary causality (sababiyya ʿadiyya), which is consistent with constant accompaniment but does not imply necessitation. The author then explains why the word 'إثبات' (establishment) was chosen over 'تحصيل' (acquisition): to indicate that the fruit (thamara) of ilm al-kalam is establishing beliefs for others, and that the beliefs themselves must be taken from the Shariʿa even if reason can grasp them. If 'تحصيل' were used, it would imply that the science's fruit is the acquisition of beliefs, which must then come from Shariʿa—but that is not the case, as the science's method is reasoning from premises. The commentary then addresses an objection that the obvious word would be 'تحصيل' because proofs and doubts are causes for the attainment of belief. The shift to 'إثبات' suggests that the fruit is not acquisition but establishment for others. The passage ends with a separate note on the phrase 'على انتفاء السببية الحقيقية' (on the negation of real causality), clarifying that restricting causality by 'real' indicates that customary causality remains, and that constant accompaniment (al-musāḥaba al-dāʾima) intended here does not negate that.

Key Terms
السببية الحقيقية
السببية العادية
إثبات العقائد
تحصيل العقائد
ثمرة الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preposition 'b' in the definition implies that proofs necessarily produce belief (real causality).
Correction: It indicates only customary causality, meaning constant accompaniment without necessitation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author choose 'إثبات' (establishment) over 'تحصيل' (acquisition) in the definition of ilm al-kalam?
A) Because acquisition is not possible for religious beliefs.
B) To indicate that the fruit of kalam is establishing beliefs for others, not acquiring them oneself.
C) Because the word 'إثبات' is more commonly used.
D) To avoid implying that beliefs come from reason alone.

Key Takeaway
The wording of kalam's definition is carefully chosen to convey that its causality is customary and its fruit is establishing beliefs for others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study 'aqeedah, do you seek to acquire certainty for yourself or to equip yourself to establish it for others? Which motive drives your efforts right now?
?
The author warns that beliefs must be taken from the Shari'a to be valid, even if the intellect could grasp them. Is there any conviction you hold that you have not honestly traced back to revelation? Will you examine that today?
?
Have you ever presented a proof for a belief assuming it would automatically produce faith in the listener, forgetting that it is only customary accompaniment? How might this affect your approach to sharing Islam with others?

Refuting al-Taftazani on 'Ithbat' as 'Tahsil'
Refutes al-Taftazani's interpretation of 'ithbat' as 'tahsil' by analyzing the referent of 'ilm'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Sanusi rejects al-Taftazani's interpretation of 'establishment' (ithbat) as 'acquisition' (tahsil) and how the definition of 'knowledge' (ilm) is key to this debate.

Core Idea
In kalam, what 'establishment' means depends on what 'knowledge' is — a verbal dispute with real consequences.

This passage is a technical refutation of a rival scholar's interpretation of a key term in the definition of ilm al-kalam. Al-Taftazani suggested that 'establishment of beliefs' could mean 'acquiring' them personally. Al-Sanusi dismantles this by analyzing what 'ilm' in the definition really refers to — is it the science's subject matter, the mental faculty, or something else? Understanding this argument sharpens your grasp of how classical scholars debated definitions with precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the overall argument. Notice the many conditional clauses — 'if ilm means this, then...'
The argument is layered; don't get lost in details yet.
2
Identify al-Taftazani's original claim
Find where the passage states al-Taftazani's view: he permits interpreting ithbat as tahsil. Understand why he thought that makes sense.
His reasoning is that establishing beliefs is acquiring them so one moves from imitation to verification.
3
Follow al-Sanusi's counter-arguments
Work through each scenario: if ilm means tasdiqat, if it means al-masa'il al-mudallala, if it means malakat al-istihsal. See how each fails or only partially works.
The key move is the distinction between malakat al-istihsal and malakat al-istihdar.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Sanusi concludes that since there's no necessity to depart from the customary meaning (malakat al-istihdar), the interpretation of ithbat as tahsil is invalid.
The argument hinges on linguistic custom and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا يجوز إلخ رد على العلامة التفتازاني حيث جوز حمل الإثبات على التحصيل وقال : معنى إثبات العقائد الدينية تحصيلها واكتسابها بحيث يحصل الترقي من التقليد أي التحقيق، ووجه دفعه أن ذلك إنما يرد لو حمل العلم على التصديقات، وكذا ملكة الاستحضار فإنها تحصل بعد العلم وتكرار المشاهدة والعلامة التفتازاني، ولو حمل على المسائل المدللة فلا شك في كون التحصيل المذكور ثمرة لها بمعنى أن من طالع تلك المسائل ووقف على أدلتها، حصل له العلم بالعقائد وعلى تقدير حمله على التصديقات، فالعلم هو التصديقات مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية المحل على ما تقرر والثمرة هي التصديقات الجزئية القائمة بالمحل على ما يشعر به لفظ العقائد، وحمله على ملكة الاستحصال كما في شرح المقاصد، يعني التهيؤ القريب بسبب حصول المأخذ والشرائط لتحصيل العقائد ففيه إنه وإن صح إطلاق الملكة عن ذلك التهيؤ لكونه كيفية راسخة لكن إطلاق أسماء العلوم المدونة، إنما هو على ملكة الاستحضار، كما صرح به في المطول ونص عليه السيد الشريف في شرح المفتاح، وصرح به كثير من الفضلاء. التحصيل؟ مع أنه قد حصل قبل الملكة ووجه الدفع ظاهر، وغاية ما يقال : إن كلاً أسماء من العلوم المدونة وإن كان يطلق على الملكة إلا أن الشائع إطلاقه على ملكة الاستحضار، وإنما حمل في تعريف الفقه على التهيؤ المذكور، لضرورة أن الأحكام العملية لا تكاد تنحصر في عدد؛ فمبلغ من يعلمها هو التهيؤ التام لها بخلاف العقائد كما أشار إليه الشارح المحقق، وإذا لم يحمل العلم ها هنا على خلاف المتعارف أعني ملكة الاستحصال لعدم الضرورة، لم يجز حمل الإثبات على التحصيل كما تحققته من سياق الكلام.
His statement: (And it is not permissible etc.) is a refutation of al-ʿAllama al-Taftazani, where he permitted interpreting 'establishment' (ithbat) as acquisition (tahsil) and said: the meaning of 'establishment of religious beliefs' is their acquisition and earning such that one ascends from blind imitation, i.e., verification. The refutation is that this would only come if one takes 'ilm' (knowledge) as affirmations (tasdiqat), and likewise the faculty of recall (malakat al-istihdar) is attained after knowledge and repeated observation. And if it is taken as the reasoned issues (al-masaʾil al-mudallala), then there is no doubt that the aforementioned acquisition is their fruit in the sense that whoever studies those issues and understands their proofs attains knowledge of the beliefs. And if it is taken as affirmations, then knowledge is the affirmations regardless of the specificity of the locus as settled, and the fruit is the particular affirmations subsisting in the locus as the wording 'beliefs' indicates. And taking it as the faculty of acquisition (malakat al-istihsal) as in Sharh al-Maqasid, meaning the proximate readiness due to obtaining the sources and conditions for acquiring beliefs, then it is said: even if the term 'faculty' (malaka) is correctly applied to that readiness because it is a settled quality, the usage of the names of the codified sciences (al-ʿulum al-mudawwana) is only for the faculty of recall (malakat al-istihdar), as stated in al-Mutawwal and affirmed by al-Sayyid al-Sharif in Sharh al-Miftah, and many scholars have affirmed it. Acquisition? While it has already been attained before the faculty, and the reason for repelling is obvious. At most it is said: each of them is a name from the codified sciences, and although it may be applied to the faculty, the common usage is for the faculty of recall. It was only applied in the definition of fiqh to the aforementioned readiness due to the necessity that practical rulings are hardly ever limited in number; so the measure of one who knows them is complete readiness for them, unlike beliefs as the verifying commentator indicated. And if 'ilm' here is not taken contrary to the customary—that is, the faculty of acquisition—due to lack of necessity, then it is not permissible to interpret 'establishment' as acquisition, as you have realized from the context of the discourse.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of al-Taftazani's view that the word 'إثبات' (establishment) in the definition of ilm al-kalam can be interpreted as 'تحصيل' (acquisition). The author explains that al-Taftazani's interpretation depends on what is meant by 'العلم' (knowledge) in the definition. If 'العلم' means the affirmations (tasdiqat) themselves, then the fruit of the science would be the acquisition of those affirmations, but that is not how the definition is framed. If 'العلم' means the reasoned issues (al-masaʾil al-mudallala), then acquisition is indeed their fruit. However, the author argues that the fruit is the particular affirmations in the mind, not the universal science. A more nuanced point is made about 'الملكة' (faculty): al-Taftazani might intend 'ملكة الاستحصال' (faculty of acquisition), but the common usage of the names of codified sciences (like 'علم الكلام') is for 'ملكة الاستحضار' (faculty of recall). The author references authorities like al-Mutawwal and al-Sayyid al-Sharif to support this. The passage also addresses an objection about acquisition occurring before the faculty, and concludes that since there is no necessity to interpret 'العلم' here as the faculty of acquisition, the interpretation of 'إثبات' as 'تحصیل' is invalid.

Key Terms
إثبات
تحصيل
التصديقات
ملكة الاستحصال
ملكة الاستحضار
المسائل المدللة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ithbat' and 'tahsil' are interchangeable terms in the definition of kalam.
Correction: Al-Sanusi shows they have distinct implications: 'ithbat' connotes establishment for others, while 'tahsil' connotes personal acquisition. The meaning of 'ilm' in the definition determines which is appropriate.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Sanusi reject al-Taftazani's interpretation of 'ithbat' as 'tahsil'?
A) Because if 'ilm' means 'tasdiqat', the fruit is particular affirmations, not acquisition.
B) Because al-Taftazani was not a reliable scholar.
C) Because the definition uses 'بإيراد' which indicates causality.
D) Because beliefs cannot be acquired.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'establishment' in defining kalam cannot be reduced to personal acquisition because 'knowledge' in the definition refers to the reasoned issues or the faculty of recall, not acquisition.

If 'knowledge' in kalam is not merely personal belief but the reasoned issues, where does your own faith stand? Are you content with imitation, or have you truly studied the proofs?
?
The author warns against interpreting 'establishment' as personal acquisition without necessity. In your pursuit of Islamic knowledge, are you seeking to 'acquire' beliefs or to 'establish' them on solid grounds? What must you change today?

Scope of the Definition and Classification of Beliefs
Clarifies the definition's scope and discusses particular beliefs, then a linguistic note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of ilm al-kalam applies to the science itself by focusing on particular beliefs and the use of its propositions as major premises for derivation.

Core Idea
The definition of kalam is about the science, not its object, and works through particular beliefs derived from its rules.

This passage addresses a subtle objection to the definition of ilm al-kalam: if the definition says one is enabled to establish beliefs, what about after they are established? The response clarifies that the definition refers to particular beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-juzʾiyya), which are continuously derived. The passage also explains how kalam's rules function as major premises, and ends with a note on a linguistic nuance in the preposition used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The text begins by stating that there is a doubt about the definition's soundness. The objection is that if the definition claims to enable establishing beliefs, it would fail once beliefs are established. Notice how the objection is framed.
2
Understand the response regarding particular beliefs
The response is that 'beliefs' here mean particular beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-juzʾiyya), not all beliefs. They are the fruit of kalam's rules. The word 'ability' (al-iqtidār) is interpreted in the usual sense of making the propositions of kalam major premises for easily obtained minor premises.
This shows the definition applies to the science, not its object.
3
Examine the counter-objection about 'God is one'
A further objection is raised: particular issues like 'God is one' would be excluded from kalam, but they are its issues. The author notes that forcing such issues into a universal form is not accepted. This highlights the nuance of what counts as a kalam issue.
4
Note the linguistic clarification on the preposition
The final part clarifies that the preposition 'ب' in 'يقتدر به' indicates assistance (istiʿāna) not causality, considering the context. This resolves a potential contradiction with other uses of 'ب'.
This is a subtle point but important for understanding the precise meaning.

His statement: (And there is no doubt in its falsehood) It may be prevented that the beliefs to which establishment is added are intended as particular beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-juzʾiyya), as evidenced by their mention in the clause of the ability (al-iqtidār) obtained by knowledge of the principles, and there is no harm in them being the fruit of the rules of ilm al-kalam. And by this it becomes clear that it is better to interpret 'ability' (al-iqtidār) in the usual sense of the validity of making those propositions major premises for minor premises that are easily attained, in order to bring the particular beliefs from potency to act. Thus the objection against the definition including other sciences is repelled clearly, because the propositions of other sciences are not suitable for that. And it is objected that the knowledge of issues whose subjects are particulars, like 'God is one', would be excluded from ilm al-kalam, even though they are among its issues. Interpreting the issue by saying 'the Necessary Existent is one' is a forced interpretation not to be adopted, so let it be considered. From his statement: (It is assistance (istiʿāna) not causality (sababiyya). The understanding of assistance in his statement 'by which one is able' is in consideration of the two contexts, so there is no contradiction between this 'ba', and the understanding of causality from the 'ba' of the two phrases.
