Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the definition of ilm al-kalam. The objection was that if the definition says one is enabled to establish beliefs, then after establishing them once, the ability is lost, making the definition non-inclusive. The response is that the 'beliefs' in the definition refer to particular beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-juzʾiyya), not all beliefs in general. The point is to show that the definition applies to the science (ʿilm al-kalam) itself, not to its object (maʿlūm). The ability (al-iqtidār) is taken in the ordinary sense: the rules of kalam serve as major premises for easily obtained minor premises, enabling the derivation of particular beliefs. This repels the objection that the definition would include other sciences, because the propositions of other sciences do not function in the same way. However, a counter-objection is raised: knowledge of issues like 'God is one' would be excluded from kalam because they are particulars, but they are among its issues. The author notes that interpreting such issues in a universal form (e.g., 'The Necessary Existent is one') is forced. The passage ends with a separate note clarifying that the preposition 'ب' in 'يقتدر به' indicates assistance (istiʿāna) rather than causality, considering the context, so no contradiction exists with the 'ب' in other phrases that indicate causality.

Meaning of al-Iqtidar and the Faculty (Malaka)
Sanusi analyzes 'iqtidar' and defends the faculty interpretation over preparedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two interpretations of 'ilm in the definition of kalam—faculty of bringing to mind vs. preparedness—and why Sanusi defends the former, along with how this relates to the concept of iqtidar (ability) as a logical faculty rather than mere acquisition.

Core Idea
The ability in kalam is a logical faculty for proof, not just preparedness.

Sanusi's commentary on the definition of kalam tackles a crucial terminological nuance. The term 'science' ('ilm) can be understood as either a firmly rooted readiness (tahayyu') or as the active faculty of bringing knowledge to mind (malakat al-istihdar). This passage explains why the latter is preferred in kalam, resolves an objection about the definition's scope, and clarifies a prepositional ambiguity. Understanding this distinction is essential to grasping the unique logical role of kalam among the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the technical terms 'malaka', 'tahayyu'', 'iqtidar'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'ilm
The passage contrasts two meanings of 'science': as a firmly rooted quality (preparedness) and as the faculty of bringing to mind. Notice how each applies to different sciences.
In fiqh, preparedness is used because rulings are unlimited.
3
Understand the objection and response
The author addresses an objection that the definition might include other sciences. The response uses the concept of iqtidar as the ability to make premises from kalam major to deduce particular beliefs.
This shows how the definition is specific to kalam.
4
Note the preposition clarification
The passage ends by clarifying that the preposition 'ba' in 'yqtadir bih' indicates seeking help (isti'ana), not causality.
This resolves a potential ambiguity.
5
Reflect on the overall takeaway
Consider why this distinction matters for the definition of kalam and its uniqueness among sciences.
The ability is a logical faculty; kalam provides the means to prove beliefs.

Obtaining the grounds and conditions for acquiring the beliefs. In it [it can be said] that even if the term 'malaka' (faculty) can be applied to that preparedness because it is a firmly rooted quality, the application of the names of the codified sciences is only to the faculty of bringing to mind (malakat al-istihdar), as is stated in al-Mutawwal and stated by al-Sayyid al-Sharif in his commentary on al-Miftah, and stated by many learned men. 'Acquisition?' While it has been obtained before the faculty? The reason for the refutation is clear. The most that can be said is that each of the names of the codified sciences, while they can be applied to the faculty, the common usage is their application to the faculty of bringing to mind. And it was interpreted in the definition of fiqh as the aforementioned preparedness, due to the necessity that the practical rulings are hardly limited in number; so the one who knows them has complete preparedness for them, contrary to the beliefs, as indicated by the verifying commentator. And if 'ilm (science) is not interpreted here contrary to the conventional meaning, i.e., the faculty of bringing to mind, due to no necessity, then it is not permissible to interpret 'proof' as 'acquisition', as I have verified from the context of the speech. His statement: 'And there is no doubt in its falsity' – it may be objected that the beliefs to which proof is attributed are meant as the particular beliefs, as indicated by their mention in the connection of the ability (al-iqtidar) obtained through knowledge of the principles, and there is no harm in them being the fruit of the rules of the science of kalam. And by this it is apparent that it is better to interpret al-iqtidar in the conventional sense of the validity of making those premises as major premises for easy-to-obtain minor premises, to bring the particular beliefs from potentiality to actuality. Thus the objection to the definition's comprehensiveness of other sciences is repelled in an obvious way, because the premises of other than our science are not suitable for that. And it is countered by the necessary exclusion from kalam of knowledge of questions whose subjects are particulars, like 'Allah is one', which is among the questions, and interpreting the question by our saying 'the Necessary Existent is one' is a forced interpretation not to be resorted to. So let this be considered. From his statement: 'It is the preposition of seeking help (isti'ana) not causality' – the meaning of seeking help in his statement 'yqtadir bih' is clear considering the two contexts, so there is no conflict between this preposition and the causal meaning from the two statements.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage discusses the interpretation of 'iqtidar' (ability) in the definition of kalam. It begins by distinguishing two understandings of 'science' ('ilm) in the definitions of the codified sciences: the faculty (malaka) of bringing to mind (istihdar) versus preparedness (tahayyu'). In fiqh, the term was interpreted as preparedness because the rulings are too numerous to fully know; one only needs the ability to derive them. In kalam, however, the beliefs are limited and fixed, so the conventional meaning of 'fine' as the faculty of bringing to mind is preferred. The author then addresses an objection that the definition might be too broad (including other sciences). The response is that the ability (iqtidar) refers to the capacity to use premises from kalam as major premises for easy minor premises, thus producing the particular beliefs. This interpretation repels the objection. The author also notes a counter-objection: that particular questions like 'Allah is one' would then be excluded from kalam, but this is dismissed as forced. Finally, the passage clarifies that the preposition 'ba' in 'yqtadir bih' indicates seeking help (isti'ana), not causality, which resolves a previous ambiguity.

The Fifth Objection: Defining the Science, Not Its Object
Sanusi presents the fifth objection and distinguishes kalam from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection to the definition of kalam and the distinction between beliefs (i'tiqadiyya) and actions ('amaliyya) in Islamic rulings.

Core Idea
The definition of kalam applies to the science itself, not its object, but beliefs are fixed while actions are unlimited.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle objection to the definition of kalam: it defines the science, not its object of knowledge. He then clarifies that what is meant by 'beliefs' (al-'aqa'id) are matters of belief, not action. This leads to a fundamental division of Sharia rulings into two categories: beliefs preserved by kalam, and actions regulated by fiqh. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the scope and purpose of each science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the fifth objection and how Sanusi responds.
2
Identify the two types of rulings
The passage divides Sharia rulings into i'tiqadiyya (belief-related) and 'amaliyya (action-related). Note how each is defined and which science deals with it.
3
Understand the contrast in scope
The passage contrasts the limitless nature of action-rulings with the fixed nature of beliefs. This explains why kalam focuses on proving beliefs while fiqh requires preparedness to derive rulings for new events.
4
Connect to the definition of kalam
Reflect on how this division supports the definition of kalam as a science that enables one to prove beliefs. The 'iqtidar' (ability) in the definition applies to these fixed beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن هذا التعريف إنما هو لعلم الكلام كما قررناه لا لمعلومه وإن أمكن تطبيقه عليه، بنوع تكلف فيقال : علم أي معلوم يقتدر ،معه أي مع العلم به إلخ ) والمراد بالعقائد ما يقصد به نفس الاعتقاد دون العمل ) فإن الأحكام المأخوذة من الشرع قسمان: أحدهما ما يقصد به نفس الاعتقاد كقولنا : الله تعالى علام قادر سميع بصير، وهذه تسمى اعتقادية ،وأصلية، وعقائد وقد دون علم الكلام لحفظها، والثاني : ما يقصد به العمل كقولنا الوتر واجب، والزكاة فريضة، وهذه تسمى عملية وفرعية، وأحكاماً ظاهريةً وقد دون علم الفقه لها، وأنها لا تكاد تنحصر في عدد بل تتزايد بتعاقب الحوادث الفعلية، فلا يتأتى أن يحاط بها كلها، وإنما مبلغ من يعلمها هو التهيؤ التام لها أعني أن يكون عنده ما يكفيه في استعلامها إذا رجع إليه، وإن استدعى زماناً بخلاف العقائد فإنها مضبوطةٌ لا تزايد فيها أنفسها، فلا تتعذر الإحاطة بها والاقتدار على إثباتها، وإنما تتكثر وجوه استدلالاتها وطرق دفع شبهاتها
The fifth: that this definition is only for the science of kalam, as we have established, not for its object of knowledge, even if it can be applied to it with some difficulty. So it is said: 'a science' i.e., any known thing, 'with which one is able' i.e., with the knowledge of it, etc.). And what is meant by 'the beliefs' (al-'aqa'id) is that which intends the belief itself, not the action. For the rulings taken from the Law are of two types: one is that which intends the belief itself, like our saying: Allah is All-Knowing, All-Powerful, All-Hearing, All-Seeing. These are called i'tiqadiyya (belief-related), and usuliyya (fundamental), and 'aqa'id (beliefs), and the science of kalam has been compiled to preserve them. And the second: that which intends action, like our saying: the witr prayer is obligatory, and zakat is a duty. These are called 'amaliyya (action-related), and far'iyya (derivative), and ahkam zahiriyya (apparent rulings), and the science of fiqh has been compiled for them. And that they are almost not limited in number, but increase with the succession of actual events, so it is not possible to encompass all of them. Rather, the one who knows them is one who has complete preparedness, i.e., he has what suffices to learn them when he returns to it, even if it takes time. Contrary to the beliefs, for they are fixed, with no increase in themselves, so encompassing them and being able to prove them is not impossible. Rather, only the ways of their proofs and the methods of refuting doubts become numerous.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection to the definition of kalam: that the definition applies to the science itself, not to its object of knowledge. The author then clarifies that the term al-'aqa'id (beliefs) specifically refers to matters of belief, not action. This leads to a division of Sharia rulings into two types: i'tiqadiyya (beliefs like Allah's attributes) and 'amaliyya (actions like prayer and zakat). The author explains that the science of kalam is dedicated to preserving beliefs, while fiqh is dedicated to actions. He further notes that actions are virtually unlimited because they increase with new events, so knowledge of fiqh is a preparedness (tahayyu') to deduce rulings, whereas beliefs are fixed and limited, so one can fully encompass them. Thus, the ability (iqtidar) in the definition of kalam applies to proving these fixed beliefs.

اعتقادي
عملي
أصول
فروع
الفقه
علم الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of kalam defines its object of knowledge (the beliefs themselves).
Correction: The definition actually defines the science itself, not its object, though it can be applied to the object with difficulty.

Checkpoint

A) It defines the science, not its object
B) It includes non-beliefs
C) It excludes actions

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's fifth objection clarifies that the definition of kalam defines the science, and the division of rulings into beliefs and actions shows that kalam deals with fixed beliefs while fiqh deals with unlimited actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about Allah's attributes, do you treat them as fixed truths that demand a response from your heart, or do you passively memorize them without letting them transform your actions? What specific attribute will you meditate on today to change how you live?
?
Sanusi says action rulings increase with new events. Are you content to rely only on what you already know, or are you actively preparing yourself to derive the correct ruling for the new situations you face? What one step can you take this week to increase your readiness in fiqh?
?
The passage distinguishes beliefs (fixed) from actions (unlimited). In your daily life, do you find it easier to focus on your beliefs or your actions? Which area do you tend to neglect, and what is one concrete way you can bring more balance between the two starting today?

Why Kalam Is Restricted to the Religion of Muhammad
Sanusi justifies specifying al-'aqa'id as related to Muhammad's religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the science of kalam is defined as dealing with religious beliefs specifically attributed to the religion of Muhammad, even though other religions have beliefs.

Core Idea
Kalam deals with Islamic beliefs, which include unique doctrines like prophethood and imamate not found in other religions.

Sanusi now addresses a potential objection to his definition: if kalam is about religious beliefs, why specify 'attributed to the religion of Muhammad'? Are beliefs not universal? This lesson unpacks the answer, showing that the restriction is necessary because Islam's creed includes distinctive tenets that other religions lack.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the parentheses indicate inserted explanations.
2
Note the inclusion of opponents
The first part says that even erroneous beliefs (like those of the Mu'tazila) are included in kalam, and their holders remain scholars of kalam. This broadens the scope.
3
Identify the objection and answer
Find where someone objects that specifying Muhammad's religion is unnecessary since beliefs are universal. Then see Sanusi's answer: the specification is needed because Islamic beliefs are manifest from his religion.
The objection is brief; the answer is fuller.
4
Understand the truth
Sanusi concludes that 'al-' in 'al-'aqa'id' indicates universality, but other religions do not contain all Islamic beliefs—especially belief in Muhammad's prophethood and imamate. Thus the restriction is meaningful.
This is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالدينية المنسوبة إلى دین محمد ( صواباً كانت أو خطأ ( فإن الخصم ( كالمعتزلة مثلاً ( وإن خطأناه ) في اعتقاده، وما يتمسك به في إثباته ( لا نخرجه من علماء الكلام) ولا يخرج علمه الذي يقتدر معه على إثبات عقائده الباطلة من علم الكلام . قوله : ( وبالدينية المنسوبة إلى دين محمد عليه السلام ) قيل تخصيص العقائد الدينية بدین محمد عليه السلام غير لازم إذ لا اختلاف في العقائد وأجيب بأنه لظهورها منه، والحق ، أن اللام في العقائد للاستغراق وليس سائر الأديان مشتملاً على جميع عقائد دين محمد عليه السلام لأن من جملتها اعتقاد نبوته عليه السلام ولوازمها ومباحث الإمامة وغيرها .
And by 'al-diniyya' (religious) attributed to the religion of Muhammad, whether correct or erroneous. For the opponent (like the Mu'tazila, for example), even if we deem him wrong in his belief and in what he adheres to in proving it, we do not exclude him from the scholars of kalam. Nor does his knowledge with which he is able to prove his false beliefs exit from the science of kalam. His statement: 'And by the religious attributed to the religion of Muhammad, peace be upon him' – it was said: specifying the religious beliefs to the religion of Muhammad is not necessary, since there is no difference in beliefs. And it was answered: because they are manifest from him. And the truth is that the definite article 'al-' in 'al-'aqa'id' is for universality, and the other religions do not include all the beliefs of the religion of Muhammad, because among them is the belief in his prophethood and its concomitants and the discussions of the imamate and others.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage addresses why the definition specifies al-'aqa'id as 'al-diniyya al-mansuba ila din Muhammad' (religious beliefs attributed to Muhammad's religion). It first notes that even erroneous beliefs like those of the Mu'tazila are included, and their proponents remain scholars of kalam. Then it presents an objection: that specifying the religion of Muhammad is unnecessary because beliefs are universal across religions. The answer given is that this specification is needed because the beliefs are manifest from Muhammad's religion, and more fundamentally, the definite article 'al-' in 'al-'aqa'id' is for universality, but other religions do not contain all the beliefs of Islam—specifically belief in Muhammad's prophethood, its implications, and discussions on imamate. Therefore, the restriction is meaningful and necessary to define the scope of the science.

Misconception: Some think that beliefs are universal across all religions, so specifying the religion of Muhammad in the definition of kalam is redundant.
Correction: Islamic beliefs include specific doctrines like the prophethood of Muhammad and the imamate, which are not found in other religions. Therefore, the restriction is necessary to define the object of kalam.

Key Takeaway

The Subject of Kalam: The Known Thing Related to Religious Beliefs
The author presents and explains the definition of the subject of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the subject of kalam as 'the known thing' in its near and distant relation to establishing religious beliefs, and how it encompasses existent, non-existent, and mode.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is everything knowable—directly or indirectly—that supports religious beliefs.

This lesson presents the foundational definition of the subject of kalam. The author clarifies that the subject is not limited to God or existence but includes all that is known (al-ma‘lūm) insofar as it serves to establish religious beliefs, whether directly as a belief itself or indirectly as a means. Understanding this scope is crucial for grasping the breadth of kalam inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic definition and its translation. Note that the subject is defined as 'the known thing' (al-ma‘lūm) considered with respect to religious beliefs.
The definition uses the phrase 'min ḥaythu yataʿallaqu bihi ithbāt al-ʿaqāʾid al-dīniyya'.
2
Distinguish near and distant relation
Identify the two types of relation: near (qarīb) when the known thing itself is a religious belief, and distant (baʿīd) when it is a means to those beliefs. The author gives examples for each.
Near relation examples include God's eternity and oneness; distant examples include atoms and void.
3
Examine the examples provided
List the examples given: direct beliefs (eternity, oneness, bodily resurrection) and indirect issues (composition from atoms, possibility of void, negation of mode, non-distinction of non-existents). See how these support the main beliefs.
The examples show the range of topics within kalam.
4
Understand the comprehensive scope
Note the final statement that the subject covers all known things: existent (mawjūd), non-existent (maʿdūm), and mode (ḥāl). This is the most inclusive definition.
This prevents limiting kalam to only existent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو (أي: موضوع الكلام) المعلوم من حيث يتعلق به إثبات العقائد الدينية تعلقاً قريباً أو بعيداً. وذلك لأن مسائل هذا العلم إما عقائد دينية كإثبات القدم والوحدة للصانع، وإثبات الحدوث وصحة الإعادة للأجسام، وإما قضايا تتوقف عليها تلك العقائد كتركب الأجسام من الجواهر الفردة وجواز الخلاء وكانتفاء الحال وعدم تمايز المعدومات المحتاج إليهما في اعتقاد كون صفاته تعالى متعددة موجودة في ذاته. فإن حكم على المعلوم بما هو من العقائد الدينية تعلق به إثباتها تعلقاً قريباً، وإن حكم عليه بما هو وسيلة إليها تعلق به إثباتها تعلقاً بعيداً، وللبعد مراتب متفاوتة. والشامل لموضوعات هذه المسائل هو المعلوم المتناول للموجود والمعدوم والحال.
And it (i.e., the subject of kalām) is the known thing, insofar as it relates to the establishment of religious beliefs, in a near or distant relation. That is because the questions of this science are either religious beliefs, such as establishing the eternity and oneness of the Maker, establishing the temporal origination and the validity of restoration of bodies, or issues on which those beliefs depend, such as the composition of bodies from indivisible atoms, the possibility of void, the negation of mode (ḥāl), and the non-distinction of non-existents—which are needed in the belief that His attributes are multiple and existent in His essence. If it is predicated of the known thing as being among the religious beliefs, its relation to establishing them is near; and if it is predicated of it as a means to them, its relation is distant, and distance has varying degrees. And what encompasses the subjects of these questions is the known thing that covers the existent, the non-existent, and the mode (ḥāl).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage provides and explains the definition of the subject of kalām. The subject is defined as 'the known thing' (al-ma‘lūm) considered with respect to its role in establishing religious beliefs ('aqā'id dīniyya), whether directly (qarīb) or indirectly (ba‘īd). The author then lists examples: direct beliefs like God's eternity and oneness, and indirect issues like the composition of bodies from atoms, the possibility of void, etc., which support these beliefs. The relation is 'near' when the known thing itself is a religious belief, and 'distant' when it is a means to such beliefs. The scope includes all categories of the known: existent, non-existent, and the mode (ḥāl).

تعلق قريب

Misconception: One might think the subject of kalam is only God or only existents.
Correction: The definition includes all known things—existent, non-existent, and modes—insofar as they relate to religious beliefs.

Defending the Definition: Objection and Response on Predicates
The author addresses an objection that the definition includes predicates and offers responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a potential objection to the definition of kalam's subject (that it includes predicates) is resolved through logical reinterpretation.

Core Idea
The objection that the definition includes predicates is answered by reinterpreting predicates as subjects.

The definition of the subject of kalam as 'the known thing insofar as it relates to establishing religious beliefs' seems to inadvertently include the predicates of the questions themselves. In this passage, the author presents an objection and two responses, refining our understanding of the definition. We will explore these logical moves step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Note the objection: the definition covers the predicates of the questions, which are also known things.
The objection is that the definition is too broad, including predicates.
2
Understand the first response: predicates become subjects
The first response argues that from the same aspect, predicates are considered subjects. This leads to an infinite regress if pressed.
This shows a logical move: changing the perspective to consider predicates as subjects.
3
Explore the second response: loose usage of 'religious beliefs'
Another solution is to say that 'religious beliefs' in the definition actually refers to the predicates themselves, used loosely. This is supported by the phrase 'if it is predicated of the known thing as being among the beliefs'.
This avoids the regress by reinterpreting the key term.
4
Consider the author's note on 'more appropriate'
The phrase 'more appropriate' is used because the definition can also be interpreted as referring to the 'positing' (waḍ‘) of the subject, meaning the imposition of beliefs on others, via deletion of a genitive.
This shows the flexibility of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقد يقال: المعلوم من هذه الحيثية المذكورة يتناول محمولات مسائله أيضاً، فالأولى أن يقال: المعلوم من حيث يثبت له ما هو من العقائد). أجيب بأن المحمولات من الحيثية المذكورة موضوعات، وإن لم تكن كذلك من جهة خصوصياتها. وأنت خبير بأنها إذا كانت من تلك الحيثية موضوعات تستدعي محمولات عليها مع انتفائها في الواقع، على أنا ننقل الكلام إلى محمولات المحمولات وهلم جرا. نعم يمكن أن يقال: المراد بالعقائد الدينية المحمولات ولو مسامحة كما يدل عليه ظاهر قوله: فإن حكم على المعلوم بما هو من العقائد، ولا يصدق المعلوم من الحيثية المذكورة على المحمولات لأنها ليست المعلوم من حيث أنه يتعلق به إثبات العقائد الدينية بل نفسها فليتأمل. قوله: (فالأولى أن يقال الخ) إنما قال فالأولى لجواز أن يصرف العبارة عن ظاهرها، ويحمل على حذف المضاف فيكون المعنى من حيث يتعلق بوضعه إثبات العقائد الدينية أي إلزامها على الغير.
His statement: 'It may be said: the known thing from this mentioned aspect also encompasses the predicates of its questions, so it is more appropriate to say: the known thing insofar as what belongs to the beliefs is established for it.' It was answered that the predicates, from the mentioned aspect, are subjects, even if they are not so from the perspective of their particularities. And you are aware that when they are subjects from that aspect, they require predicates upon them, despite their non-existence in reality, and we then transfer the discussion to the predicates of predicates, and so on. Yes, it can be said that by 'religious beliefs' the predicates are meant, albeit by looseness, as indicated by the apparent meaning of his statement: 'For if it is predicated of the known thing as being among the beliefs'—and the known thing from the mentioned aspect does not apply to the predicates, because they are not the known thing insofar as it relates to the establishment of religious beliefs, but rather they themselves. So ponder. His statement: 'It is more appropriate to say...' He only said 'more appropriate' because it is permissible to divert the expression from its apparent meaning and to assume the deletion of the genitive, so the meaning becomes: insofar as its positing relates to the establishment of religious beliefs, i.e., obligating them upon others.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the definition of the subject of kalām. The objection states that the definition (the known thing insofar as it relates to establishing beliefs) also includes the predicates of the questions, because they are also known things. The response is that from that same aspect, these predicates are considered subjects, not predicates—they are subjects that require further predicates. The discussion then shifts to an infinite regress. An alternative solution is to say that 'religious beliefs' in the definition actually refers to the predicates themselves, using loose terminology. The author notes that the phrase 'more appropriate' is used because the definition can be reinterpreted as referring to the 'positing' (waḍ‘) of the subject, meaning the imposition of beliefs on others.

Key Terms
محمولات
موضوعات
حذف المضاف
مسامحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of kalam's subject is flawed because it includes predicates.
Correction: The definition is not flawed; it can be reinterpreted so that predicates are either considered subjects or are referred to loosely as 'religious beliefs'.

Checkpoint

C) It does not specify the method of argumentation

Key Takeaway

The Subject of Kalam: The Known and Its Two Relations to Beliefs
Defines the subject of kalam as the known and explains its two relations to beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject of kalam is defined as 'the known' (al-malum) and how it relates to religious beliefs through close and distant attachments.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is the known, covering all that can be known, related to beliefs either directly or indirectly.

Before diving into specific theological arguments, one must know what the science of kalam is about. The author defines its subject as 'the known' (al-malum), a broad category that includes existents, non-existents, and states. This definition sets the stage for discussing religious beliefs and the means to prove them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a feel for the author's dense style. Pay attention to the key terms.
The passage is from the beginning of the first 'observatory' (al-marṣad al-awwal).
2
Identify the key definition
Find where the author states that the encompassing subject is 'the known' and note that it covers the existent, the non-existent, and the state.
This definition is crucial because it determines the scope of kalam.
3
Understand the two types of attachment
The passage distinguishes close attachment (taʿalluq qarīb) and distant attachment (taʿalluq baʿīd). Close means the known itself is part of a religious belief; distant means it is a means to establish one.
The distance can have varying degrees.
4
Consider the objection and refinement
The passage raises an objection that the known also includes the predicates (conclusions). The refinement narrows the definition to 'the known insofar as it is established for it that which is from the religious beliefs', i.e., considered as something to which a belief is attributed.
This answers the objection and provides a precise definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول - المرصد الأول : فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم وجواز الخلاء وكانتفاء الحال، وعدم تمايز المعدومات المحتاج إليهما في اعتقاد كون صفاته تعالى متعددة موجودة في ذاته والشامل لموضوعات هذه المسائل هو المعلوم المتناول للموجود والمعدوم والحال فإن حكم على المعلوم بما هو العقائد الدينية تعلق به إثباتها تعلقاً قريباً، وإن حكم عليه بما هو وسيلة إليها تعلق به إثباتها تعلقاً بعيداً، وللبعد مراتب متفاوتة وقد يقال : المعلوم من هذه الحيثية المذكورة يتناول محمولات مسائله أيضاً، فالأولى أن يقال : المعلوم من حيث يثبت له ما هو من العقائد
The First Standpoint - The First Observatory: On what must be presented in every science. And [the issues of] the possibility of void (al-khala'), the non-existence of state (al-hal), and the non-distinctness of non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) needed in the belief that His attributes, exalted, are multiple and existent in His essence. And that which encompasses the subjects of these issues is the known (al-malum), which encompasses the existent, the non-existent, and the state. For if a judgment is made on the known insofar as it is part of religious beliefs, then the affirmation of them attaches to it in a close attachment (ta'allaq qarib); and if a judgment is made on it insofar as it is a means to them, then the affirmation of them attaches to it in a distant attachment (ta'allaq ba'id), and the distance has varying degrees. And it may be said: the known from this mentioned respect also encompasses the predicates of its issues. So it is more appropriate to say: the known insofar as it is established for it that which is from the religious beliefs.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of kalam. It begins by stating the heading: 'The First Standpoint - The First Observatory: On what must be presented in every science.' Then it lists some specific issues—possibility of void, non-existence of state, non-distinctness of non-existents—needed for the belief that God's attributes are multiple and existent in His essence. The passage asserts that the subject encompassing all these issues is 'the known' (al-malum), which covers the existent, the non-existent, and the state. It then distinguishes two ways the known relates to religious beliefs: either the known is itself part of a religious belief (close attachment, ta'allaq qarib), or it is a means to establishing a religious belief (distant attachment, ta'allaq ba'id), with the distance having varying degrees. The passage raises an objection: the known from this respect also includes the predicates of its issues (i.e., the conclusions). The preferred response is to define the subject as 'the known insofar as it is established for it that which is from the religious beliefs'—i.e., the known considered as something to which a religious belief is attributed, whether directly or indirectly.

Key Terms

العقائد الدينية
تعلق قريب
تعلق بعيد
الخلاء
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The subject of kalam is only the divine essence and attributes.
Correction: The subject of kalam is broader: it is the known (al-malum), which includes not only the divine but also existents, non-existents, and states, insofar as they relate to religious beliefs.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam is the known (al-malum), which encompasses all that can be known, and it relates to religious beliefs either directly (close attachment) or indirectly (distant attachment).

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Resolving the Objection: Predicates as Subjects from the Relevant Respect
Objection and answer: predicates from the relevant respect are subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the known includes its predicates is resolved by showing that predicates are subjects from the relevant perspective, and that 'religious beliefs' refers to those predicates.

Core Idea
From the relevant respect, predicates are subjects—so the known does not include them as predicates.

This passage addresses a critical objection to the definition of kalam: if the subject is the known, then the predicates (the religious beliefs themselves) would also be known and thus part of the subject. Sanusi offers a nuanced answer: from the specific respect of being related to religious beliefs, the predicates are actually subjects. This lesson unpacks that answer and shows how it preserves the definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the opening line: 'And it may be said: the known from this mentioned respect also encompasses the predicates of its issues.' This is the objection: if the subject is the known from a certain respect, then the predicates (the religious beliefs) are also known, so they would be part of the subject.
The objection threatens the coherence of defining kalam as knowledge of religious beliefs.
2
First Answer: Predicates as Subjects
The answer states: 'the predicates from the mentioned respect are subjects, even if they are not so from the perspective of their particularities.' Notice the shift in perspective: from the respect of being related to religious beliefs, the predicates function as subjects (things that have other predicates attached).
This does not change the subject of kalam; it reinterprets the role of predicates.
3
Address the Infinite Regress
The answer notes that if predicates are subjects, they require predicates themselves, leading to an infinite regress. But the regress is not problematic because we can 'transfer the discussion to the predicates of predicates, and so on.' This shows the regress is harmless.
The infinite regress is merely a logical consequence, not a contradiction.
4
Second Answer: Lax Interpretation
Alternatively, the author says 'what is meant by religious beliefs is the predicates, even by way of laxity.' This means the term 'religious beliefs' directly refers to the predicates, so the known does not need to include them as predicates—they are the beliefs themselves.
This is supported by the phrase 'insofar as it is from the beliefs' in the original definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد يقال : المعلوم من هذه الحيثية المذكورة إلخ) أجيب بأن المحمولات من الحيثية المذكورة موضوعات، وإن لم تكن كذلك من جهة خصوصياتها، وأنت خبير بأنها إذا من تلك الحيثية موضوعات تستدعي محمولات عليها مع انتفائها في الواقع، على أنا ننقل الكلام إلى محمولات المحمولات وهلم جرا نعم يمكن أن يقال : المراد بالعقائد الدينية المحمولات ولو مسامحة كما يدل عليه ظاهر قوله : فإن حكم على المعلوم بما هو من ولا يصدق المعلوم من الحيثية المذكورة على المحمولات لأنها ليست المعلوم من حيث أنه يتعلق به إثبات العقائد الدينية بل نفسها فليتأمل.
His saying: 'And it may be said: the known from this mentioned respect also encompasses the predicates of its issues, etc.' The answer is that the predicates from the mentioned respect are subjects, even if they are not so from the perspective of their particularities. And you are aware that if they are subjects from that respect, they require predicates upon them, while they are non-existent in reality. Moreover, we transfer the discussion to the predicates of predicates, and so on. Yes, it can be said: what is meant by 'religious beliefs' is the predicates, even by way of laxity, as indicated by the apparent meaning of his saying: 'For if a judgment is made on the known insofar as it is from the beliefs...' And the known from the mentioned respect does not apply to the predicates, because they are not the known insofar as the affirmation of religious beliefs attaches to it, but rather they are themselves [the beliefs]. So let it be examined.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection (quoted from the matn) says: 'the known from this mentioned respect also encompasses the predicates of its issues'—i.e., if the subject is the known, then the predicates of the issues (the conclusions) are also known, so they would be part of the subject, which is problematic. The answer clarifies: from the mentioned respect (i.e., insofar as the known is related to religious beliefs), the predicates are actually subjects—they are the things to which other predicates attach. Even though from their own particularities they are predicates, from this relational perspective they function as subjects. The answer further notes that these 'subject-predicates' themselves require predicates, leading to an infinite regress, but that is not an issue because the discussion can be shifted to the predicates of predicates, etc. Alternatively, one can say that by 'religious beliefs' the author means the predicates themselves, in a loose sense, as indicated by the original phrase 'insofar as it is from the beliefs'—the predicates are not the known from that respect, but rather the beliefs themselves. Thus the objection is resolved.

Key Terms
المحمولات
الحيثية المذكورة
موضوعات
العقائد الدينية
مسامحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that answering the objection requires redefining the subject of kalam (e.g., to exclude predicates entirely).
Correction: The answer does not redefine the subject; it merely reinterprets the perspective: from the relevant respect, predicates are subjects, or 'religious beliefs' refers to predicates directly.

Checkpoint
How does the author first resolve the objection that the known includes its own predicates?
A) By denying that predicates are known
B) By stating that from the mentioned respect predicates are actually subjects
C) By changing the definition of the subject to the divine essence
D) By conceding that the objection is valid and revising the definition

Key Takeaway
The objection is resolved by recognizing that from the relevant respect, predicates are subjects, and 'religious beliefs' can refer to those predicates loosely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection raised a logical problem that seemed to undermine a definition you rely on. When you face objections to your faith that seem logically airtight, do you immediately feel shaken, or do you pause to ask: 'From what respect is this objection coming?' Will you commit to examining the perspective behind objections before letting them unsettle your conviction?
?
The author offers two answers, the first involving a harmless infinite regress. How often do you accept explanations that involve infinite chains or mysteries in your faith? Does your comfort with such concepts reveal a trust in Allah's wisdom, or is it a sign of intellectual laziness? Today, take one doctrine that puzzled you and ask: 'Could an infinite regress be harmless here?'
?
The second answer uses 'laxity' (musamaha) in language to resolve the problem. In your own life, are you rigid with the words of scholars or the Qur'an, or do you allow room for contextual understanding? Could your insistence on literal precision be closing you off from deeper truths? Pause and ask: 'Where am I demanding certainty where the scholars allowed flexibility?'

Subject of Kalam: Concept or Instances?
The initial objection and counterargument about the subject being instances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject of kalam cannot be taken as the instances of 'the known' and how essential accidents can be more specific than their subject.

Core Idea
The subject of a science is its concept, not its instances.

In defining the subject of kalam, scholars debated whether it is the concept of 'the known' or its individual instances. This lesson explores the initial objection that if the subject were the instances, it would be too broad, making investigated properties non-essential. The response clarifies that essential accidents can indeed be more specific.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Examine the Arabic passage and translation, noting the objection and its counterargument.
2
Understand the objection
Why does taking instances as the subject make it too general? Because instances are more general than the concept.
3
Grasp the counterargument
The defender points out that an essential accident can be more specific than its subject, so the objection fails.
4
Reflect on the implication
Appreciate how this discussion shapes the precise definition of kalam's subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن أريد به ما صدق عليه من إفراده كان أعم منه فلا يكون أيضاً عرضاً ذاتياً مبحوثاً عنه، ما لم يقيد بما يجعله مساوياً له، كما حقق في موضعه لأنا نقول : قد حقق هناك أيضاً أن العرض الذاتي يجوز أن يكون أخص من معروضه نعم يتجه أن الحيثية المذكورة لا مدخل لها في عروض القدرة للمعلوم مثلاً، قوله : ( نعم يتجه إلخ ) يعني أن الحيثية من تتمة الموضوع، فيجب أن يكون لها مدخل في عروض الأحوال لتكون أعراضاً ذاتية للمقيد، ولو لم يكن لها مدخل تكون الأحوال عارضة لمطلق الموضوع، فتكون من جملة الأحوال الغريبة للمقيد ضرورة أن المقيد أخص من يعد قوله : ( وإن أريد به ما صدق عليه من إفراده كان أعم منه فيه بحث، وهو أنه يمكن أن ) ما يصدق عليه مفهوم العلم، بحيث يتناول كل ما يساوي شيئاً من المحمولات حتى أن مفهوم المعلوم من جملة ما صدق عليه، وما يساويه هو الوحدة والماهية مثلاً، وحينئذ لا اتجاه بما ذكره، ويمكن أن يدفع بأن هذا التوجيه يوجب أن بعض المعلومات تارةً، من موضوعات الكلام وتارة من أنواعها وهذا تعسف لا طائل تحته فليتأمل .
His saying: 'And if what is meant by it is what its individuals apply to, they would be more general than it, so it would not be an essential accident (arad dhati) investigated, unless it is restricted to what makes it equal to it, as has been verified in its place.' For we say: It has also been verified there that the essential accident is allowed to be more specific than its subject. Yes, it is apparent that the mentioned respect has no role in the occurrence of power (qudra) to the known, for example. His saying: 'Yes, it is apparent, etc.' means that the respect is part of the subject, so it must have a role in the occurrence of states (ahwal) so that they are essential accidents for the restricted [subject]; and if it had no role, then the states would be accidental to the absolute subject, and they would be among the accidental states (ahwal ghariba) for the restricted [subject], necessarily because the restricted is more specific than what is included. His saying: 'And if what is meant by it is what its individuals apply to, they would be more general than it' has a discussion, namely that it is possible that what the concept of knowledge applies to is such that it encompasses everything that equals something of the predicates, even to the extent that the concept of the known is among what it applies to, and what equals it is oneness (al-wahda) and quiddity (al-mahiya), for example. In that case, what he mentioned is not valid. And it can be pushed back by saying that this interpretation necessitates that some known things are sometimes from the subjects of kalam and sometimes from its types, and this is a forced [interpretation] with no benefit under it, so let it be examined.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage discusses whether the subject of kalam is the concept of 'the known' (al-malum) or its instances (individuals). It begins with an objection: if the subject is the instances, it would be more general than the concept, so the investigated accidents (like 'being a means to belief') would not be essential accidents. The defender counters that essential accidents can be more specific than their subject. Then a nuance is introduced: the mentioned respect (i.e., being related to beliefs) is part of the subject, so it must have a role in the arising of states; otherwise those states would be accidental. Another objection is raised: if the subject is what the concept applies to, it might include things like oneness and quiddity, which are not investigated. The answer suggests that this would lead to forced consequences, so the preferred view is that the subject is the concept of the known taken with the respect. The passage ends with a call for careful examination.

Key Terms
الأفراد
العرض الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The subject of kalam must be the instances of the known (like Allah, atoms).
Correction: The passage shows that taking instances would make the subject too broad; the subject is the concept with a specific respect.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection claim that taking instances as subject prevents essential accidents?
A) Instances are too specific
B) Instances are more general than the concept
C) Instances are not real
D) Essential accidents cannot be general

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam is the concept of the known, not its instances, because essential accidents can be more specific than their subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your understanding of Allah's attributes as a concept to be studied or as instances to be experienced? Which approach does this lesson argue for, and how would a misstep affect your certainty in faith?
?
Have you ever confused the subject of a science with its instances in your own thinking? How might that error lead you to overlook essential accidents that are more specific than the concept itself?

The Role of the Respect (Haythiyya) in Essential Accidents
The condition that the respect must be part of the subject, and a final objection-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the respect (haythiyya) must be part of the subject for states to be essential accidents, and how a later objection is resolved.

Core Idea
The respect is integral to the subject; without it, states become accidental.

After establishing that the subject is the concept, a nuance arises: the respect (like being related to beliefs) must be included in the subject itself. Otherwise, states like 'being a means to belief' would be accidental to the unrestricted subject. This lesson unpacks that condition and addresses a further objection about the scope of the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Examine the Arabic and translation focusing on the role of the respect.
2
Understand the condition
The respect must be part of the subject for states to be essential accidents; otherwise, they are foreign states.
3
Examine the further objection
An objection arises: if the concept applies broadly, it might include oneness and quiddity. The response is that this would lead to forced categorization.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why the preferred view is that the subject is the concept with the specific respect.

Objection and Reply on the Subject as the Concept of Known
Objection and two replies regarding whether the subject of kalam is the concept of known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars defend the definition of the subject of kalam against an objection regarding the concept of 'known'.

Core Idea
Essential accidents can be more specific than their subject; the subject includes a specific consideration.

This lesson presents an objection to the definition of kalam's subject as the concept of 'known' and two scholarly replies. The objection argues that predicates would not be essential accidents. The replies clarify the nature of essential accidents and the role of the 'consideration' in the subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the objection and replies.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يقال' and 'لأنا نقول' which mark the objection and response.
2
Identify the objection
Find the two alternatives presented in the objection: if 'known' is taken as a concept, predicates are more specific; if taken as individuals, predicates are more general.
The objection concludes that in neither case are predicates essential accidents.
3
Understand the first reply
The first reply, introduced by 'لأنا نقول', asserts that essential accidents can indeed be more specific than their subject, as already established.
This directly counters the first alternative of the objection.
4
Understand the second reply
The second reply explains that the subject includes a 'consideration' (حيثية) which restricts it, making predicates essential accidents to the restricted subject.
This addresses the issue of generality and shows why the consideration is integral.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : إن أريد بالمعلوم مفهومه فأكثر محمولات المسائل أخص منه فلا يكون عرضاً ذاتياً له، وإن أريد به ما صدق عليه من إفراده كان أعم منه فلا يكون أيضاً عرضاً ذاتياً مبحوثاً عنه، ما لم يقيد بما يجعله مساوياً له، كما حقق في موضعه لأنا نقول : قد حقق هناك أيضاً أن العرض الذاتي يجوز أن يكون أخص من معروضه نعم يتجه أن الحيثية المذكورة لا مدخل لها في عروض القدرة للمعلوم مثلاً، قوله : ( نعم يتجه إلخ ) يعني أن الحيثية من تتمة الموضوع، فيجب أن يكون لها مدخل في عروض الأحوال لتكون أعراضاً ذاتية للمقيد، ولو لم يكن لها مدخل تكون الأحوال عارضة لمطلق الموضوع، فتكون من جملة الأحوال الغريبة للمقيد ضرورة أن المقيد أخص من يعم قوله : ( وإن أريد به ما صدق عليه من إفراده كان أعم منه فيه بحث، وهو أنه يمكن أن ) ما يصدق عليه مفهوم العلم، بحيث يتناول كل ما يساوي شيئاً من المحمولات حتى أن مفهوم المعلوم من جملة ما صدق عليه، وما يساويه هو الوحدة والماهية مثلاً، وحينئذ لا اتجاه بما ذكره، و يمكن أن يدفع بأن هذا التوجيه يوجب أن بعض المعلومات تارةً، من موضوعات الكلام وتارة من أنواعها وهذا تعسف لا طائل تحته فليتأمل .
It is not said: If by 'known' its concept is meant, then most of the predicates of the issues are more specific than it, so it would not be an essential accident to it; and if by it what it applies to of its individuals is meant, then it is more general than it, so it would also not be an essential accident investigated, unless it is restricted by what makes it equal to it, as has been verified in its place. For we say: It has been verified there also that the essential accident can be more specific than its subject. Yes, it is directed that the mentioned consideration has no role in the accrual of power to the known, for example. His statement: '(Yes, it is directed, etc.)' means that the consideration is part of the subject, so it must have a role in the accrual of states to be essential accidents to the restricted, and if it had no role, the states would be accidental to the absolute subject, thus they would be among the extraneous states to the restricted, of necessity because the restricted is more specific than the general. His statement: '(And if by it what it applies to of its individuals is meant, it is more general than it' has a discussion, and it is that it is possible that) what applies to the concept of knowledge, such that it encompasses everything that equals something of the predicates, even the concept of known is among what it applies to, and what equals it is unity and quiddity for example, and then there is no direction to what he mentioned. And it can be repelled by that this orientation necessitates that some known things are sometimes among the subjects of kalam and sometimes among its species, and this is a far-fetched assumption with no benefit under it, so reflect.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and two replies. The objection argues that if the subject of kalam is the concept of 'known', then most predicates are either more specific (if the subject is the concept) or more general (if the subject is individuals), so they would not be essential accidents. The first reply, introduced by 'for we say', asserts that essential accidents can indeed be more specific, as established. The second reply refutes a potential objection by showing that the alternative leads to absurdity, as some things would be both subjects and species of kalam. The commentary also explains that the 'consideration' (haythiyya) is part of the subject and must have a role in the accrual of states to avoid them being extraneous.

مفهوم
عرض ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An essential accident must be equal in scope to its subject.
Correction: The passage shows that an essential accident can be more specific than its subject, as established in logic.

A) Because the concept of known is impossible.

D) Because the concept leads to circularity.

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam is not merely the concept of 'known' but includes a specific consideration, allowing essential accidents to be investigated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to engaging with objections on their own terms before responding?
?

In your own life, where might you be demanding perfect alignment where a more nuanced relationship (like essential accident being more specific) actually holds? Will you reflect on one such area today and adjust your thinking?
?
The passage shows that adding a 'consideration' (حيثية) can transform a relationship from extraneous to essential. Is there a relationship in your life—with your Creator, with others, or with your own self—that seems superficial because you haven't identified the specific consideration that makes it meaningful? Will you seek that consideration and act upon it?

Why the Subject of Kalam is the Concept of the Known
A defense of the concept of known as subject and explanation of departure from Hawaashi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject of kalam is defined as the concept of 'known' rather than the essences of known things, and the rationale for departing from an alternative methodology.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is the concept of the known, not the known things themselves.

This passage addresses a critical methodological choice in defining the subject matter of kalam. After establishing that the subject is the concept of the known, the author anticipates objections concerning the nature of accidents and states, and explains why he departs from the approach taken in the glosses on Sharh al-Matali'. The discussion illuminates important principles about essential accidents and the structure of the science.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
Identify the objection that accidents belong to essences, not concepts, and see how the author redefines 'subject' as considering things under the description of being known.
Notice the shift from concrete individuals to a descriptive concept.
2
Understand the departure from Hawaashi
See why the author rejects the method of making subject the types of existents, because it would include God's essence and cause a problem.
This is a key methodological decision.
3
Grasp the two conditions for essential accidents
Learn the two conditions that an essential accident must meet to be specific to its subject, and how they apply to the states in later stations.
These conditions ensure proper scientific investigation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لأنا نقول : قد حقق هناك أيضاً هذا اختيار للشق الأول من الترديد، فإن قلت : العوارض والأحوال المبحوث عنها ليست أعراضاً وأحوالاً لمفهوم المعلوم، بل لذاته فكيف يختار أن موضوع العلم مفهوم المعلوم؟ قلت معنى كونه موضوع العلم أن الملحوظ وصف المعلومية على معنى أنه يبحث في الكلام عن أعراض ما اتصف بمفهوم المعلومية من حيث هو كذلك بلا ملاحظة خصوصية فرد وذات له، المعلومية فإن قلت : قد اختار في حواشي شرح المطالع أن موضوع الحكمة أنواع الموجودات واعتبر تقييد المحمولات العامة بما يجعلها مساويةً للموضوع، فلم عدل ها هنا عن تلك الطريقة ؟ واختار أن الموضوع مفهوم المعلوم، قلت : وجه العدول أنه لو كان الموضوع ذوات المعلومات كان ذات الواجب من جملة الموضوعات، فيرد الوجه الثاني من النظر الذي أورده على كون موضوع الكلام ذات الله تعالى بقي فيه بحث، وهو أن جواز خصوص العرض الذاتي بمعروضه مشروط بأمرين أحدهما : الشمول والمساواة مع مقابله الذي يتعلق بهما عرض علمي والثاني : أن لا يحتاج في عروضه إلى أن يصير الموضوع نوعاً معيناً لا حقيقياً، ولا إضافياً كما صرح به في حواشي شرح المطالع، والأحوال المبحوث عنها في الموقف الثالث والرابع الخامس يحتاج في عروضها للمعلوم إلى أن تصير عرضاً، أو جوهراً أو واجباً كما يدل عليه سياق كلامه في بيان وجه ترتيب الكتاب على ستة مواقف فليتأمل.
His statement: '(For we say: It has been verified there also) this is a choice for the first part of the disjunction. For if you say: The accidents and states investigated are not accidents and states of the concept of known, but of its essence, so how can it be chosen that the subject of the science is the concept of known? I say: The meaning of it being the subject of the science is that what is considered is the description of being known, meaning that it is investigated in kalam about the accidents of what is described by the concept of being known insofar as it is such, without consideration of the specificity of an individual and its essence, the concept of being known. Then if you say: He chose in the glosses on Sharh al-Matali’ that the subject of wisdom is the types of existents and he considered restricting the general predicates to what makes them equal to the subject, so why did he depart here from that method and choose that the subject is the concept of known? I say: The reason for the departure is that if the subject were the essences of known things, then the necessary Being would be among the subjects, and then the second aspect of the consideration that he brought forth regarding the subject of kalam being God’s essence would apply. There remains a discussion, which is that the permissibility of the specificity of the essential accident to its subject is conditioned by two things: one is comprehensiveness and equality with its opposite which relates to a scientific accident, and the second is that its accrual should not require that the subject become a specific type, neither real nor relational, as stated in the glosses on Sharh al-Matali’. And the states investigated in the third, fourth, and fifth stations require in their accrual to the known that they become accident, substance, or necessary being, as indicated by the context of his speech in explaining the reason for ordering the book into six stations. So reflect.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defends the choice of the concept of known as the subject of kalam. It answers an objection that accidents belong to essences, not concepts, by explaining that the subject is considered only under the description of 'being known', not specific individuals. It then addresses why the author departed from the method used in Hawaashi Sharh al-Matali’, where the subject was types of existents. The departure avoids including God's essence in the subject. The passage also states two conditions for an essential accident to be specific to its subject: (1) comprehensiveness and equality with its opposite, (2) the subject should not need to become a specific type. Finally, it notes that the states in the later stations require the known to become accident, substance, or necessary being.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The subject of kalam must be the essences of known things, not a concept.
Correction: The subject is the concept of known under the description of being known, because accidents and states are considered without regard to specific individuals.

Checkpoint
Why did the author depart from the method used in Hawaashi Sharh al-Matali’?
A) To make the subject more specific.
B) To avoid including God's essence in the subject.
C) To follow the method of earlier scholars.
D) To make the predicates equal to the subject.

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam is the concept of known, considered under the description of being known, which avoids including God's essence and meets the conditions for scientific investigation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you ever treat Allah as just another object of investigation, forgetting that He is beyond the descriptions we apply to created things? How can you ensure your approach remains humble and does not reduce Him to a subject of analysis?
?
The author insists that the subject of kalam is the concept of 'known' under a description, not the essences themselves. Have you ever mistaken your conceptual understanding of something for the reality itself? In what area of your faith might you be making this mistake?
?
Consider the two conditions for essential accidents: comprehensiveness and not making the subject a specific type. Are there intellectual commitments you hold that force Allah into a category (e.g., substance or accident) that He transcends? What would it mean for you to let go of such categories in your worship?
